==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Three window solutions were designed to improve the thermal performance of windows.
Two frameless windows were created, one fixed frameless window with ventilation hatch.
The other frameless window was a sliding window, partly fixed and partly openable as
a slidable element. The third window solution was a Reference pivot window with an
improved slim window frame made of Glass Fibre Reinforced Polyester (GFRP). All
windows were designed with quadruple glazing as the desire was to replace triple glazing
with quadruple glazing as the main glazing product.
All three models were modelled and its thermal properties calculated. COMSOL
multiphysics was used to model the heat transmission in the models. The primary in-
vestigation was to find the thermal linear losses between the glazing and wall for the
different intersections. The weighted average linear loss around the frameless window-
hatch model was calculated to be 0.0432 W/mK. Whereas around the sliding window, it
was calculated to be 0.0389 W/mK. In the COMSOL modelling, the calculation of the
total U-value of the Reference window gave 1.66 W/m2K. The average weighted linear
loss around the Reference window was 0.023 W/mK.
The Indoor climate was investigated with regards to overheating and draught. An
low energy NZEB residential building was used for all dynamic simulations. The thermal
requirements of BR18 and DS/EN 15251 of overheating for residential buildings were
used. This meant a target of maximum 100 hours a year of 27◦C indoors. Draught was
avoided by applying an airflow rate of 6 Air Change per Hour (ACH) as the maximum
limit. Differently coated glazed buildings were simulated with IDA ICE to find the over-
heating and airflow rates for a whole year in Denmark. Overheating and draught was
avoided with quadruple glazed windows, despite not using solar coated glazing units.
This was due to an effective ventilation setup combing the mechanical ventilation to-
gether with a smart opening of windows or hatch. Another reason was the use of glazing
units with higher Light Transmission, which meant smaller glazing units with high day-
light intake but lower radiation area.
The window solutions were likewise implemented in building models to investigate
the energy use. The frameless window solutions and the Reference window were mod-
elled with the its most important properties. This included the weighted average linear
loss, glazing and window dimensions together with the glazing properties. The models
were compared to the buildings with more conventional windows. The results indicated
that replacing the conventional model with a quadruple glazed Reference window could
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
reduce the space heating by 29%. Whereas comparing the glazing influence to the heat-
ing power, a quadruple glazed Reference window would improve it by 15% compared
to with a triple glazing. It was further demonstrated, that replacing the conventional
windows with the frameless window-hatch and sliding window would reduce the space
heating by 50% and 53%, respectively.
By conducting a Cost analysis of the insulation, it was demonstrated that the win-
dows alone could have certain impact to the insulation cost. A default NZEB building
with insulation thicknesses of 370 mm in floor, 365 mm in roof and 300 mm in the walls
was used as a default model comparison. The Reference model with quadruple glazing
managed to reduce the insulation cost by 35%, whereas the frameless window-hatch and
sliding models reduced the total cost by 39% and 41%.
From the results it could be concluded that the use of glazing units with more layers
could help reduce heat losses, and possible to acquire quadruple glazing units with rel-
atively high Light Transmission. Foremost it was shown that frameless windows could
reduce heat losses by large margins, which in return would save space heating in the
buildings. Furthermore, it was illustrated that this would also have economical benefits
to both the window production and the cost of other elements of buildings.
Keywords: Frameless, ventilation hatch, sliding window, improved window frame

Preface
This M.Sc thesis was prepared at the department of Building Energy at the Technical
University of Denmark in fulfilment of the requirements for acquiring a Master of Science
degree in Civil Engineering.
Firstly and foremost, I would like to express my greatest gratitude to my supervisor
Professor Svend Svendsen for his great help during the whole process. His commitment
to the project and his extensive knowledge was crucial to the project.
Secondly, I would also like to thank my Co-supervisor, Researcher Kevin Michael Smith
for his time to share his knowledge about simulation tools and building services.
Then I would like to thank my fellow student and friend Elizabeth Saliba for her bene-
ficial cooperation between her own project and mine. I would like to thank her for the
great help and support she showed me throughout the project. I would also like to thank
her for the 3D illustrations of the finished windows.
Lastly, I would like to thank my family and friends for their great support. You supported
me during the whole project, especially during difficult times. I owe you everything for
your support and encouragement.
Kongens Lyngby, February 17, 2019
Hajan Farhan

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Background
Several implementations have been made to reduce the energy consumption of building.
This include measures done by active means, addressing ventilation, heating and cooling
components. The greatest efforts have been to reduce the energy consumption in build-
ings by passive means. This involves the construction parts, where building components
such as walls, roof, floor and windows have been improved. This has been done by re-
ducing or eliminating heat losses and thermal bridges found in the various construction
parts. New material types has been used, new concepts of building envelopes have been
developed and different approaches of constructing have all greatly helped reduce the
energy demand of modern buildings and renovated buildings alike. This has already
been achieved in numerous countries worldwide and is as for today a fundamental part
of buildings and construction.
An obstacle not fully solved, is the concept of windows and window frames in particu-
lar. Although, as the energy performance of windows has been improved by introducing
windows with multiple glass layers. The current best available window solution is a
3-layer glazing, normally implemented into modern buildings. The heat losses of those
are still far bigger than those found in other construction parts. This is the main reason
of focusing on better insulating windows, as it is documented in preliminary studies,
indicating that 50% of the room heat demand can be removed by improving windows.
An illustrative example is the comparison between the thermal transmittance (U-value)
of a facade and the window in it, which in some cases mounts to a value 15 times smaller
than that of a window [7]. This has created the approach of further reducing the energy
losses by either increasing the number of layers in a glazing units or focus on low-energy
window frames and even the concept of frameless windows.
Removing the current frame type and create frameless windows would introduce a
new way of constructing windows. The frame is directly integrated in the facade element
and the glazing unit is glued directly to the wall. This has inspiration from previous
studies in both fixed windows and openable window-facade concept, respectively [38]
[31]. This could help create new low energy windows and promote new methods of win-
dow incorporation into facades.
==PAGE:22==
1 Introduction
Studies from research labs have emerged, presenting the possibility of implementing
4, 6 and even 10 layer glazing as promising solutions to reduce heat losses in windows.
Nevertheless, four bigger problems emerge, firstly the amount of space needed to im-
plement multiple layer glazing. Secondly, large thermal bridges in the frame structures.
The largest portion of heat losses in modern buildings is caused by the thermal bridges
from windows. The third issue is the weight, increasing number of glazing layers result
in heavier windows. The last issue is lower light transmission, a result of each additional
glass sheet which would imply larger glazing units to compensate the daylight intake [31].
1.2
Aim of thesis
The aim of the project is therefore to create two new frameless low-energy window
concepts for future residential buildings.
The project will address two frameless window concepts, a frameless ”sliding window”
and a ”frameless window integrated with a ventilation hatch”. The issues and obstacles
faced with the designs will be addressed to achieve the desired window designs. The
ideas have potential to change and affect both the energy consumption in buildings but
also the building industry itself by the production of windows. This could in return have
economic benefits and reduce time in the window production.
1.2.1
Window characteristics
Ideas and designs will be created through drawings. These will be validated by analysis
of the capacities and limitations.
The windows will be modelled by the advanced multiphysic tools COMSOL. The results
from the computational modelling from COMSOL are implemented in IDA ICE, the
windows are then simulated dynamically to see the influence of the windows on indoor
environment and energy use. Those results are used for the economical analysis to see
the economical benefits by reducing the heat losses and using the proposed window.
With these programs the models can be analysed mathematically, specifically for:
• Mechanics: Forces, loads, displacement in terms of implementation in the various
constructions. This involve the glazings and the whole window structure.
• Optical performance: Sunlight, solar radiation, moisture in the glazing and its
components.
• Heat transfer: Heat losses, through glazing, from thermal bridges, analysis of
key areas.
• Energy balance: Energy use, heat balance
• Economics: Cost benefits, reduction in insulation.
2
==PAGE:23==
1.3 Reading guideline
1.3
Reading guideline
It is expected prior to reading that the reader has basic knowledge of heat transfer,
window construction and other relevant subject. The structure of the thesis consists of
11 chapters and additional chapters for appendices.
• Chapter 1 introduces the reader to the background of the subject and the dilemmas
and objectives surrounding it.
• Chapter 2 covers analysis of former literature on the subjects related to the core
of the thesis. The analysis is used to gather more knowledge about the subjects.
To further develop on those ideas or consider a different approach to achieve the
aim of the thesis.
• Chapter 3 covers the thermal properties of glazing units together with an analysis
of the thermal performance.
• Chapter 4 is about the mechanics of the window units. The design and assembly
of the new proposed concept are presented.
• Chapter 5 describes the computational modelling of the window models to find
the thermal performance of the whole window models.
• Chapter 6 addresses the simulation modelling of the windows and the reference
building used to conduct the dynamic simulations.
• Chapter 7 is the analysis of the indoor climate by addressing the overheating in
relation to the window design and glazing units.
• Chapter 8 is the building energy analysis of the window models.
• Chapter 9 mentions the benefits and potentials of these new concepts in an eco-
nomical sense.
• Chapter 10 provides an illustrative presentation of the finalised window solutions.
• Chapter 11 is the conclusion of the project.
All relevant appendices are attached in the end of the project.
3
==PAGE:24==
4
4
==PAGE:25==
CHAPTER 2
Literature study
This chapter will look into the preliminary studies conducted on frameless windows and
the implementation of multiple layers of glazing in a window.
Several efforts have been made to reduce energy losses caused by the window frame
and glazing alike. The most successful attempt at reducing heat losses in windows, has
been by improving the energy performance through glazing by applying more layers
of glass. Another approach has been to create new glass types, for instance insulated
glass (IG), Anti-Condensation, and Solar Control Coating (SCC). These glazing units
have helped energy-wise with addition to reducing risk of condensation and reducing
overheating. The bigger problems in terms of energy losses have been with the window
frame, where efforts have been made to make the frames slimmer but also use alternative
frame materials with lower thermal conductivity than the conventional frames.
In one of the studies, David Appelfeld et al.(2015) developed a window frame of
the material type Glass fibre reinforced polyester (GFRP) in 2010. The main attributes
of GFRP was its significantly lower thermal conductivity than those of metallic frame
materials such as aluminium and steel. Another important property was the strength of
GFRP compared to other low conductivity materials as PVC and wood. As stated by
Appelfeld, thanks to the strength of the material it was possible to have a slimmer frame
without further reinforcement required. The durability of GFRP was also better due
to no absorption of moisture, no corrosion or degrade by UV-radiation and therefore
no need for expensive maintenance.
Out of the different window frame designs pro-
posed, the best performing window frame with a thickness of 44 mm had a U-value of
1.42 W/m2K and a total U-value of 0.76 W/m2K for a window size of 1230 x 1480 mm [3].
Despite the good results, the U-value is still considerable larger than that of a facade.
GFRP Frame proved capable enough to withstand some of the mechanical stresses and
with a fine durability. Additionally, with current technology, an energy efficient frame
would still cause large thermal bridges making it unfeasible to fulfil future building
regulations. This is one of the reasons to move towards alternative solutions, such as
the application of frameless windows. In those cases, energy losses can be reduced by
50% if frameless windows were to be successfully implemented [25].
==PAGE:26==
2 Literature study
2.1
Frameless window with quadruple glazing
The window concept is based on the research project ”MEM4WIN” presented in EeB
PPP Project Review 2016. The project is part of a collaborative research project funded
under the 7th Framework Programme for Research and Technological Development of
the European Commission [31]. The presented concept was an effort to create a more
energy efficient window for zero-energy buildings. The window is a quadruple glazing
window containing thin glass membranes [35], which has helped reduce weight by 50%
and cost by 20% with an U-value of 0.3 W/m2K [31]. Figure 2.1 and Figure 2.2 show
the glazing unit and its properties and the application of the window into the facade
respectively.
Figure 2.1: Model of quadruple glazing
window and properties [13].
Figure 2.2: Frameless window concept in-
corporated into facade [13].
A significant contribution from the MEM4WIN research has been the new way of
hardening glass panes. The new hardening technology makes it possible to create glaz-
ing units with layers of thin glass membranes. The advantages of the glass type are
many due to its properties. As stated by the resulting report, the glass membranes have
”lower breakage losses due to thin glass sheets of enormous strength and flexibility”
[13]. This cause lower distortions in facade in relation to climate loads. Including lower
stress on spacer bars and lower loads on fittings and frame thanks to its light weight [13].
It is known by several studies and investigations that windows account for 30-50% of
transmission losses through building envelopes [41]. Much of this transmission losses in
sealed windows is influenced by the edge seal and its components. A study conducted by
Jeff Baker for WestLab Canada, investigated the thermal performance of spacer. Baker
found that the thermal conductivity of the spacer affects the heat losses of the frame,
edge-of-glass and the total U-value of the window [4]. In the study he presented, how
the effective thermal conductivity (λeff) of the spacer influences the total U-value of
6
==PAGE:27==
2.1 Frameless window with quadruple glazing
the window. If the conductivity is above 2 W/mK, the U-value curve flattens out. It is
therefore only necessary to look at spacer material with a thermal bridge lower and for
future cases significantly lower than 2 W/mK.
Study by Sofie Van Den Bergha et al. 2012 addressed the function and property of
the edge seal and its components such as spacer bar, sealant and desiccant of its time.
Van Der Bergha concluded that improvement in the thermal performance of edge seal
could be achieved by reducing the heat transfer width of it, reduce its thermal conductiv-
ity and increase the thickness of the edge seal [41]. Furthermore, she more specifically
stated that the width of the spacer bar should be decreased and a secondary sealant
would help reduce the thermal bridge at the edge of the glass [41].
In another study related to the design of a different edge seals from 1997, Michael
Löffer & Doris Buck investigated the use of foamglass as spacer material. What makes
foamglass unique is the low thermal conductivity of around 0.04-0.055 W/mK [27]. Löf-
fer & Buck writes that foamglass as edge seal help omit the need for secondary seal,
the material is resistant to high pressure making it suitable for mechanical use but its
tensile strength on the other hand is quite low [27]. It is also vapour and gasproof and
compatible with sealant materials [27]. Foamglass has its limitations which makes it a
uncertain material to work on but it was proven by the study that it could in theory be
used.
Another important aspect is the acoustical properties of windows. The main sound
problem for buildings is the noise from traffic and air crafts outside. Studies has shown
that the connections of windows, between frame and wall, glazing and frame have large
influence to the structural vibration and sound transmission [24]. Lower frequencies is
controlled by the building components which show that sound transmission from 100
Hz can only be reduced by the design of the window [24]. The spacer design is highly
influential to this but the concept of spacer design is similar to that of heat losses. The
solution is also to have low conducting spacer of an appropriate material [15].
All studies stressed the importance of the different components to the thermal per-
formance of the window. The quadruple glazing window can be improved by addressing
the more detailed structure of the window. The variety of materials is big and should
therefore be investigated to achieve the best possible quadruple glazing window.
This window concept is an alternative type which seeks to completely eliminate the
window frame by incorporating the glazing directly to the facade. The window frame is
removed which implies that the spacer profile would remain together with the glazing.
In that way the heat losses between the facade and the window frame would be removed.
Jørgen M. Schultz provided a good example of how such a concept could look in his
study ”Ramme-/Karmkonstruktioner til højisolerende vinduer” from 1992. His idea was
to incorporate the glazing unit deep into the wall, typically in between the insulation
layer of the building envelope without significantly disrupting the insulation properties
7
==PAGE:28==
2 Literature study
of the insulated wall. This design would therefore remove the thermal bridge caused by
the spacer as it is in ”contact” with the insulation [38].
Another solution from Schultz was to ”interrupt” the thermal bridge found hori-
zontally (through the depth of the window).The method was to implement insulation
thickness corresponding to the one-dimensional heat resistance at the middle of the pane.
This helped significantly reduce the thermal bridge with the depth of the window. The
results indicated that the best performing windows were those with wider insulation
thickness and deeper incorporation of the glazing unit. Schultz also stated that these
factors had no influence on the transparency of the window but would mean larger glaz-
ing units. Furthermore, Schultz recommended the use of gases like krypton and aerogel
instead of air in cavities, which from the results indicated a significant reduction of
the window’s U-value. This statement complies with another investigation conducted
by Abdelsalam Aldawoud in 2017, which found that Argon and Krypton improves the
energy performance of a window by 15% and 20% respectively compared to air [2].
The two best results achieved with the fixed window in Schultz’s project were win-
dows with a total U-value of 0.5 W/m2K for a double glazing of 4 mm and a 20 mm
cavity of aerogel. The other one was also a double glazing of 4 mm and a cavity 0.5 mm
of vacuum providing a U-value of 0.53 W/m2K, despite of a small total thickness at 8.5
mm. These results indicated that the heat losses from the window frame were minimal
or entirely gone, hence the total U-value of the window was almost equal to that of the
window glazing [38]. Figure 2.3 is the illustration of Schultz concept of a fixed frameless
window.
Figure 2.3: Frameless window concept placed into wall [38].
8
==PAGE:29==
2.2 Non-sealed multi-pane window
2.2
Non-sealed multi-pane window
In a more recent study, a similar concept of a non-sealed multi-pane glazing window was
covered in Diana Lauritsen’s PhD thesis ”Durability of future energy efficient building
components”. It was stated that to avoid ”puncture” of glazing unit, the glazing has
to be non-sealed which in return would imply air in the cavities instead of gasses [26].
As aforesaid, this would mean a decrease in the energy performance of the window but
applying multi-panes and increasing the width of air cavities would increase the insula-
tion effect because the heat is led through a longer profile, which in return increases the
thermal performance [26]. Lauritzen further stated that it would also imply a further
need for connection between outside air and air cavities, which in return requires an air
filter to filter out dirt, moisture and allow airflow [26].
Lauritzen pointed out that some of the problems of a non-sealed window were the
prospect of internal condensation and proper transparency. But the most significant
obstacle was the risk of cracks in the glazing due to air expansions from thermal differ-
ences. Measures to combat these problems were taken in the form of implementing tubes
into the cavities of the window, shown in both Figure 2.4 and Figure 2.5. This would
level out low and high pressure from the cavities and also counter thermal expansion to
thereby avoid cracks in the glazing [26].
Figure 2.4: Proposed
concept
of
non-
sealed window, with tubes con-
necting to cavities [26].
Figure 2.5: Drainage tubes in fixed win-
dow concept [38].
Lauritzen conducted experiments involving three prototypes of the proposed window
in her PhD project. For two of the windows, the outer pane was uncoated whereas the
outer pane of the third window was coated with anti-reflection. All three window types
experienced internal condensation, but it was less notable for the window with anti-
reflection. The condensation appeared at the bottom part of the outer cavity, which
9
==PAGE:30==
2 Literature study
Lauritzen indicated was caused by the moisture from outside entering the cavity faster
than the air cavity in the glazing could dry itself out. Her solution was to implement
drying remedy in the air filter (tubes), which would help extract the moisture of the
outside air before entering the cavities. The drying remedy had the desired effect and
helped remove the internal condensation, although the drying remedy only had an esti-
mated lifetime of one year [26].
A fixed window would remove the possibility of ventilating by a open window. This
function is necessary in residential buildings to maintain a good Indoor Air Quality
(IAQ) while have as little overheating as possible. Some companies have already created
a ”ventilation hatch” which is applied either above or next to the window. This small
hatch can also be modified to filter the air coming from outside from moisture, particles
and also be acoustical insulated. However, with the current state of the hatch has some
limitations. The most significant limitation is the low thermal performance of exisiting
hatch products.
With current technology the ventilation hatch is capable of sufficient ventilation but
constitutes a new area of heat losses. In his master thesis from 2018, Mattia Capraro in-
vestigated the required radius of such ventilation hatch to achieve the desired air change.
His results showed that the radius had to be of a great size, but not as big as the windows
[8]. The larger the size of the hatch, the larger the heat losses would be experienced
through it.
It could be solved with mechanical ventilation but this would on other hand result in
increased energy use. A passive solution in the window system has to be found until a
modern ventilation is capable of providing enough ventilation without too large pressure
losses.
Another problem with multi-pane windows is that the light transmittance (LT) is
reduced by increasing the number of glazing. This creates obstacles when applying 4
layer glazing or more to a window. The windows have be to be larger to compensate for
the LT but this would in return imply heavier windows and also larger window surface
area which would mean larger heat losses.
2.3
Other window concepts
Along the mentioned concepts, some other types involving different types of insulated
glazing and glazing material exist. These subjects have potentials to improve the energy
performance of windows but the research on the matter is lacking behind compared to
the aforementioned concepts and ideas in the previous sections.
10
==PAGE:31==
2.3 Other window concepts
2.3.1
Vacuum Insulated Glazing (VIG)
The idea of using vacuum for insulating purposes is not a new idea, neither is the concept
of Vacuum Insulated Glazing (VIG). Research on VIG started around 20-30 years ago.
An example was the double glazing unit with Vacuum presented in Schultz’s study in
1992. This VIG concept was fixed to the wall construction, resulting in a U-value of
0.51 W/m2K [38].
However, it was only recently it became a realistic alternative glazing unit due to
more research indicating the possibility of making it a future commercial window prod-
uct.
Only heat by radiation can be transferred through vacuum which means large
amount of heat losses can be saved through the glazing. Vacuum insulation could also
be a great benefit to sound transmission in buildings, the absence of molecules in vacuum
means that sound cannot be transmitted through the glazing. This could reduce noise
from outside by a great margin [6].
VIG has great potential to replace the conventional windows with its small size and
its energy efficiency as well as acoustical properties. The average U-value of the VIG is
around 0.5 W/m2K for a double glazing unit, although the actual thermal transmittance
varies for different studies. Theoretically speaking, a U-value as low as 0.15 W/m2K is
possible for VIG [2]. Nevertheless, some new and different problems follow. The produc-
tion of VIG is different to that of other windows. It is manufactured by removing the
gas or air between glass sheets and assembled at room temperature in a vacuum cham-
ber [22]. The vacuum gap between the glass sheets results in big air pressure difference
between the inside vacuum state and outside ”atmospheric” air [6] [22]. Metal pillars
are implemented through the window to sustain the pressure differences and avoid the
breakage of the glazing. The problem of this is that the thermal conductivity of the
pillars is much greater than that of glass [30]. Which in the end results in a reduction
in the radiative heat transport around the pillars [30]. Former studies have addressed
that the magnitude of this is small enough to be ignored in most cases.
The life time of a VIG is limited to around 5 to 10 years depending on the pro-
duction quality. The process of assembling VIG is complex and expensive, therefore
only affordable if the thermal insulation is kept for tens of years. A recent study by K.
Chuntonov et al. from 2018 investigated the possibility of increasing the life time of VIG.
Chuntonov addressed the problems associated with VIG is generally due to the getter.
A getter is described as a ”deposit of reactive material placed inside a vacuum system,
for the purpose of completing and preserve the vacuum”. Chuntonov used different types
of getter materials and a different approach. The conventional getter materials were
replaced with reactants which only contained chemically active metals with no thermal
activation required [10]. Measurements of sorption was conducted for the new materi-
als, showing the capacity to replace the convetional getter materials. An activationless
getter was installed under vacuum after the edge sealing of the window and its thermal
degassing [10]. Calculations of the lifespan was conducted as a function of ”number of
11
==PAGE:32==
2 Literature study
charged into getter housing granules” showing that increasing the number from 10 to 80
could increase the lifespan of the VIG from 5 to 40 years.
Purpose of the project is not to investigate VIG but its potentials cannot be ignored.
Replacing current window systems with VIG could more the less become a future reality.
The recent study by Chuntonov et al. (2018) presents some potential solutions to VIG
systems, which could help introduce VIG into the market of modern energy efficient
windows.
2.3.2
Dynamic glazing
This technology allow the materials used for glass sheets in windows to adapt to the
current condition. More specifically it involves the glazing to respond independently
to the presence of natural stimuli as light or heat [9]. Marco Casini (2016) mentioned
that photochromic glazing (adaptation to light) or thermochromic glazing (heat) could
be used as dynamic passive glazing. This could help reduce overheating during summer
and allow more lighting during winter.
However as Casini states, problems of late response time, inefficiency and small
operational time makes it difficult introduce the type of glazing [9]. More research and
optimisation of the current knowledge is needed before it should be addressed as an
reasonable design.
12
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 3
Glazing unit properties
This part covers the properties of the glazing and window with regards to the transmittance
of solar radiation. It will continue with the discussed designs and its components from
the previous chapter. These parts are addressed in terms of heat transfer and moisture
to analyse light, heat losses, radiation and condensation.
3.1
Optical and radiative properties
The solar radiation reaching the window of a room is divided between the Light Trans-
mission (LT) as daylight and solar radiation (g) as heating.
The radiation is either
transmitted (τ), absorbed (α) or reflected (ρ) when reaching the glass pane, like the
balance illustrates in Equation 3.1. The typical illustration of the spectral distribution
of the sunlight in a glazing unit is shown in Figure 3.1. The last variable, qi is the
internal, secondary heat heat transfer.
Figure 3.1: Distribution of sunlight reaching glazing unit.
τ + α + ρ = 1
(3.1)
The light and solar radiation is together with the thermal insulation, the focus of
this project. In Figure 3.2 the range of the visible part of the solar radiation known as
==PAGE:34==
3 Glazing unit properties
the light transmittance (τv) and the heating part, the solar direct transmittance (τe) is
presented.
Figure 3.2: Spectral distribution of solar radiation [34], visible part known as sunlight.
Glass manufacturers have developed Insulated Glass (IG) with low-emission coating
to help reduce losses through the middle of the glass. Other types include solar coating
control (SCC) to reduce the solar radiation and thereby help reduce overheating. An-
other product is the use of anti-condensation which as its name reflect, helps reduce
the risk of anti-condensation. The glass used for this project were all found from glass
manufactures. Pilkington and Saint-Gobain were the primarily ones.
The following parts explain the fundamentals and basics about the thermal and
optical properties of glass, glazings in buildings.
3.1.1
Light Transmission
The visible part of the sunlight, ranged in wavelengths from 380 nm to 780 nm, is
important for the well beings of occupants outside and inside. It has been proven by
various studies that sufficient amount of daylight is necessary for the comfort of the
residents. This is why daylight is crucial in buildings. The light transmission through
the glazing depends on the type of glass pane.
The light transmittance is according to standard EN410 expressed as Equation 3.2 [14].
τv =
∑780nm
λ=380nm Dλτ(λ)V (λ)∆λ
∑780nm
λ=380nm DλV (λ)∆λ
(3.2)
14
==PAGE:35==
3.1 Optical and radiative properties
Where Dlambda is the relative spectral distribution of illuminant D65, found in EN410
[14]. V (λ) is the spectral luminous efficiency for photopic vision defining the standard
observer for photometry. And ∆λ is the wavelength interval, corresponding to an inter-
val of 10 nm.
τ(λ) is the spectral transmittance of the glazing unit. The reflection and transmit-
tance in a insulated glass unit with n number of layers is illustrated by Figure 3.3 and
formulated as Equation 3.3. The principle is dependent on number of glass panes and fol-
low the same pattern when number of glass panes exceed double glazing. Double glazing
is expressed similarly but without the last part of the denominator in the equation.

The light reflectance is expressed with some minor differences compared to Equa-

3.1.2
Total solar energy Transmittance
The solar energy transmittance is reflected by Equation 3.4.
τe =
∑2500nm
λ=300nm Sλτ(λ)∆λ
∑2500nm
λ=300nm Sλ∆λ
(3.4)
Where Sλ is the relative spectral distribution of the solar radiation, presented in
EN410, the remaining factors were the same for Equation 3.2.
Glass manufactures use the acronym g-value to describe the total solar energy trans-
mittance. The definition of the g-value is reflected as the fraction of solar radiation
transmitted through the glazing unit into the room. The g-value is valid for solar radia-
tion perpendicular on the glazing, it is formulated as Equation 3.5.
g = τe + qi
(3.5)
15
==PAGE:36==
3 Glazing unit properties
Where qi is the internal heat transfer factor and τe is the direct solar energy trans-
mittance, previously presented. The internal heat transfer is dependent on the heat
transfer coefficients towards the inside and outside and the solar direct absorptance
(αe). Nonetheless, for multiple glass panes follows other considerations as well, but this
can be found in the standard EN410 [14].
3.1.3
Cavity
Different combinations of gas cavities are available on the market. The most common
options are Argon, Krypton and dry air. Krypton and Argon is usually mixed with
small amount of air. The thickness of the cavities is normally in the range of 12 mm to
20 mm, sometimes reaching cavities of 30 mm or more.
In general, it is thermally wise to increase the thickness and number of cavities to
increase the thermal performance. However, this would be an unwise decision for struc-
tural reasons. This is explained in the next chapter. The width of the window and a
combination of thick cavities and coated glass panes could also reduce the light trans-
mission. This would in return require larger window areas to provide sufficient daylight.
The use of vacuum in cavities could change this problem. Radiation is the only type
of heat transmitted in vacuum which would significantly help reduce heat losses through
glazing. Sound cannot travel in vacuum either, but this is covered in later parts of the
project. The default thickness of a vacuum cavity is 0.5 mm but can reach a maximum
of 1 mm. This would help create thinner glazing units capable of reducing the heat
losses more than the default glazing products on the current market.
3.2
Glazing performance
The glazing performance was investigated for different cavity gases, thicknesses, different
glass panes and coatings and in the end for different types of Vacuum Insulated Glazing
(VIG).
The goal of the investigations was to simulate different combinations to find the
optimal glazing with a low U-value and high light transmission. The lower the light
transmission is, the larger or the more glazing units is needed to achieve sufficient day-
light indoor. The size would in return affect the heat losses through the window as it is
dependent on the area. This makes it essential to find a glazing type offering both high
light transmission while maintaining a reasonable size with a low U-value.
16
==PAGE:37==
3.2 Glazing performance
3.2.1
Description of investigations
The default window for this project was considered to be a quadruple glazing unit. The
default glazing type was designed with inspiration from the prototype presented as part
of the MEM4WIN research [13]. Two similar examples with different purposes, one for
with solar coating and the other with Anti-Condensation feature was designed with Pilk-
ington glass.
Figure 3.4: Default quadruple glazing unit [13].
The two glazing examples, one with SCC and one without is shown with its prop-
erties on the two next pages. Both had U-values of 0.35 but it can be seen that use of
SCC has a more significant influence to the g-value and LT alike. The outer pane was
of the type Suncool 70/35, with a very efficient emission coating to reduce heat losses
and solar radiation. The inner panes was of the same type for both glazing, 4 mm thick
Optitherm S3 (toughened). The cavity was consistently the same material (Argon 90%)
and thickness (18mm).
Another thing that should be noted is the fact that the second glass pane from the
outer glass pane could be replaced with a normal non-coated glass pane. Removing the
coating on that glass pane could slightly increase the Light Transmission coming through
without changing the thermal performance of the glazing unit. The coated glass pane
could be replaced with Opticlear or Optiwhite from Pilkington. In this project all glass
panes were coated as means of investigation, however this fact was mentioned for future
references.

3.2 Glazing performance

==PAGE:40==
3 Glazing unit properties
Pilkington Spectrum was used to design quadruple glazing with its layers and find
the properties of design of choice [33]. However, Spectrum in its current state is limited
to quadruple glazing (4 layer). The performance of glazing with more than 4 glass panes
was simulated with Window 7.6 by Berkeley Lab. The calculation methods of both
program was based on the European standards of EN410 for optical and thermal perfor-
mance and EN673 for calculating the U-value. This was partly explained in section 3.1.
The reminder of the program is based on matrix optics and heat transport in anisotropic
in-homogeneous media. These parts are not explained in this project as it belongs to
other topics.
The setup in Window 7.6 is different to Spectrum with a variety of glass by different
glass manufactures. The model is dependent on the environmental conditions, which for
this project was defined according to the default settings used by the European Com-
mittee for Standardization (CEN). The tilt of the glazing was set to be 90◦and both
the length and height was set at the default value of 1 m.
3.2.2
Investigation of optimal cavity
The cavity thickness and its influence to the U-value of the glazing is dependent on
the gas cavity and the thickness of it. A quadruple glazing, similar to the default one
with Anti-condensation was investigated for all available cavities according to Pilkington
Spectrum, from 6 mm to 28 mm. The three main types of glazing cavities on the market,
pure Air, 90% Argon and 90% Krypton were all investigated with Pilkington Spectrum.
Figure 3.5 show the relation between the thermal conductivity of the gas cavity and
the thickness to the U-value of the glazing. The U-value for all three cases experienced
a exponential decrease when the cavity thickness was increased by the first steps. The
curve continues with a similar rate of drop for Air and Argon, but stops around 20 mm
and flattens out from 22 mm and upwards, indicating that the optimal cavity thickness
was 22 mm for both Air and Argon.
Krypton with lower conductivity experiences a smaller decrease but follows a similar
but ”shorter” pattern compared to air and argon. It can be seen that the curve flattens
out and experiences a small increase in U-value with an increasing thickness from 14
mm and upwards. The optimal cavity thickness for Krypton was found to be 14 mm.
The U-values of the glazing with the optimal cavity thickness was 0.4 W/m2K with Air,
0.31 W/m2K with Argon and 0.29 W/m2K with Krypton.

The results from Figure 3.5 show the importance of using the proper cavity thickness
and material. The lowest U-value was not achieved by the largest thickness but is rather
dependent on the thermal conductivity of the cavity material. Glazing with cavities of
Argon and Krypton performs better. On the other hand, the use of those in cavities
is more expensive than with dry air. In addition to this, the proper thickness should
be used to avoid under- or overdimension the cavity space in the glazing unit. The
width of the cavity should also be considered in relation to the structurally capacities
of the window as aforementioned in chapter 4. This would help decrease the risk for
displacement of glass panes in the insulated glazing unit, whilst reducing the total width
of the unit.
3.2.3
Investigation of Light Transmission
This investigation was performed with Pilkington Spectrum to find the highest possible
Light transmission for a quadruple glazing unit. Only annealed glass panes were used for
the investigation, with a maximum thicknesses of 4 mm to avoid thick and heavy glazing
units. Laminated glass panes were not addressed to minimise cost and not considered
a preferable glass type for this project. All products were found from Pilkington. The
primary factors affecting the outcome of the Light Transmission were glass type, coating
and thickness of glass pane.
21
==PAGE:42==
3 Glazing unit properties
The cavity was defined as Argon 90% with a width of 22 mm for all cases. As afore-
mentioned a glass thickness of 4 mm (max) or thinner was the preferred choice due to
weight but also to avoid decreasing the light transmission. To accommodate a glazing
with low U-values, the three inner panes of the quadruple glazing, 4 mm Optitherm
S3 OW 1 was used. This glass type and coating functions as thermal insulation and is
an ideal choice for neutral light transmission and in insulating glass units, according to
Pilkington. The changing parameter was the outer pane, as reflected by the X-axis in
Figure 3.6.

3.2 Glazing performance
The investigated glazing units can be seen in Table 3.2, defined according to their

and thickness follows, with the next glass pane and its coating. All glazing presented in
the table were investigated with results in Figure 3.6. All investigated glazing products

Figure 3.6: Light transmission for different coated glass panes with Optitherm S3.
As shown in Figure 3.6, the U-value was almost identical with a range of 0.31 to 0.32
W/m2K. The inner panes were mostly influential to the U-value and to some degree the
g-value. The outer pane was highly influential to the outcome of the LT. The highest LT
achieved was by the use of neutral glass types. This was seen with OptiWhite, Optifloat
Clear and Optitherm S3 OW with LT of 71%, 70% and 70% respectively. These types
still have high g-values which is undesirable for south oriented rooms due to risk for
overheating.
This made it relevant to address the LT with solar coating. The highest possible LT
while using Solar coating is about 62% while the g-value of such glazing is about 0.34,
the product used was Suncool 70/35 OW. The results indicate that the LT is affected in
case the focus is to bring down the solar radiation. Therefore a compromise has to be
met to find the optimal glazing for its respective purpose. The thermally insulated glass
panes were capable of reaching a relatively high LT and g-values below 0.5, which could
work if implemented but need verification. Further investigations involving overheating
hours, cooling energy and energy use were needed to determine the optimal quadruple
glazing. This was conducted in chapter 7 and chapter 8.

3.2 Glazing performance
3.2.3.2
Investigation of Vacuum Insulated glazing
As aforementioned, the use of Vacuum in glazing could be a new and alternative way of
insulating glazing even further. This makes it an interesting concept of consideration.
Two types of VIG were investigated in terms of number of glass panes, from triple to
10 glass panes glazing. Suncool 70/40 Pro (6 mm) by Pilkington was used as the outer
layer for a glazing with low emission coating. Optitherm S3 AC (4 mm) by Pilkington
was used for cases involving Anti-condensation or self cleaning function. The remaining
glass panes in the glazing units was of the type Optitherm S3 Pro (4 mm). The thickness
of the vacuum was set to 1 mm for all cases, the thickness of the vacuum had marginal
influence to the properties. The main influence to the properties was caused by the types
of glass panes, cavity and the number of cavities with vacuum. According to Window
7.6 the effective thermal conductivity of Vacuum is 0.0004 W/mK, whereas the density
and heat capacity was zero.

As it can be seen from Figure 3.7 and Figure 3.8, the three main properties of the
glazing showed a correlation to the number of glass panes and the number of vacuum
cavities. As expected the highest values were present at lower number of panes, with a
steady decrease along an increasing number of panes.
It can be seen that the U-value can be drastically reduced while keeping a reasonable
g-value and LT. A glazing unit with more than 8 glass panes can achieve a U-value below
0.1 W/m2K, close to that of a wall. This would require a large glazing area or several
glazing units to meet the daylight requirements, as the LT was strongly reduced by an
increasing number of glass panes. The most realistic option could be a triple or even
quadruple glazing. The triple glazing had an U-value as low as 0.347 W/m2K and LT
around 65% and 71% with and without SCC.

==PAGE:46==
3 Glazing unit properties
The results indicates the great potential of using Vacuum as cavity in glazing. How-
ever, higher light transmission and thinner glass panes is needed to make it reality. The
same thing concerns the construction and lifetime of VIG, which is limited with the
current technology.
It should further be noted that metallic support pillars in the edges of the glazing
is required to bear the atmospheric load on the glass panes when using vacuum in the
cavities. These pillars are generally made of high conducting metals, ceramics, and glass
which could cause additional heat losses. This was not taken into account as the focus
was to find the centre values of the glazing. The final U-value would a bit larger but it
should be stated that these pillars only measure 0.5 mm in diameter but are separated
by a distance of 20-30 mm for each.
3.3
Moisture & mass transfer
Another aspect of the window design is the influence of moisture and air transfer to
the structure. The edge seal is placed around the glazing to create a barrier to prevent
the infiltration of water vapour. The sealant in sealed units is necessary to prevent gas
diffusion from the cavities and also provide moisture protection. Whereas the desiccant
helps prevent condensation inside the glass surfaces of the glazing.
The prospect of condensation on the outer surfaces of the glazing is also an important
aspect. A worst case scenario involving condensation could be as follows: ”The glazing
starts with condensation on the glass surface during morning due to a cold night, with
a sudden change of outdoor temperature and intense solar radiation could heat the glass
panes to a point of breakage and puncture”. It is of highest importance to avoid this
scenario, as the damage and expense would be devastating.
One method to estimate the occurrence of surface condensation is by calculating the
Temperature Ratio Value (TRV or fsi) by Lombardi & Aghemo (1989) as reflected by
Equation 3.6 [28]. However this requires that the various humidity, outdoor temperature
at a given inside temperature is known.
TRV = ϑSi −ϑe

(3.6)
Where ϑ is a symbol for the temperatures, e represents outdoor, i is indoor and Si
is an expression for the inside surface.
The outdoor temperature ϑe can by same formula be determined by Equation 3.7
ϑe(= ϑec) = TRV · ϑi −ϑe

CHAPTER 4
Mechanics & assembly of
glazing
This chapter addresses the mechanics of a glazing and its components. The materials
and properties will be investigated. The two default window designs were ”Frameless
window with quadruple glazing” and ”non-sealed multi-pane window”.
4.1
Components
The typical modern glazing unit consist of multiple glass panes, structurally held together
along the perimeters by edge seal [41]. The main purpose of the edge seal is to keep the
glass panes separated at equal distances while providing a barrier to prevent infiltration
of water vapour or exfiltration of the gas (or dry air) fill between the panes [41]. Spacer
bar, sealant, dessicant all constitute the edge seal of an glazing. An illustration of the
edge seal and its components in a glazing unit is presented in Figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Example of double glazing unit with components of edge seal [12].
==PAGE:50==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
4.1.1
Glass pane
The glass panes in the windows were used for optical and thermal purposes and also
structurally. It was obviously necessary to consider the strength of the glass to address
potential breakage caused by the design or the glazing components. A good perspective
of the structural capacity of the glass panes is described by a stress-strain curve as the
one shown in Figure 4.2. Many of the glass properties can be relieved by the curve. It
was also obvious from the curve that the glass would collapse into scatters in case of
fracture and thereby loose its strength. All types of glass including annealed, tempered
and laminated exhibits an ideal linear elastic behaviour until it reaches breakage.
Glass panes in a multi-layer glazing unit with cavities in between are more exposed
to mechanical stresses. One example is the deformation of the glazing unit when exposed
to higher temperatures, another one could be large temperature difference between the
mid-pane and pane surface of the inner glass pane. This is more thoroughly explained
in section 4.2.
The properties of the glass presented in Table 4.1, were taken from the same study,
Haldemann et al.(2008). The values remain similar with small variations, except when
the glass material is organic or with a specific function. The optical and thermal prop-
erties of glass panes is covered in the next chapter.
Table 4.1: Glass properties according to Haldemann [18].
Density (ρ)
Thermal conduc-
tivity (λ)
Poisson ratio (µ)

30
==PAGE:51==
4.1 Components
The thickness of the glass panes is dependent on the number of glass panes. Multi-
pane glazing requires thicker glass panes on the outer pane and the inner pane, whereas
the mid panes can be thinner. The thicker ”outer” panes are neccessary to transport
the wind forces from outside and withstand the forces.
4.1.2
Spacer bar
The Spacer is implemented to hold the glass panes at a fixed distance from each other,
this defines the interpane space which will be filled with dry air or an inert gas [41]. The
spacer design is highly influential to both the thermal and the acoustical performance
of a window. Especially the thermal conductivity of the material is highly influential to
these performances. Low conducting spacers or ”warm-edge spacer” are made of mate-
rials such as composite (fibre-glass), silicone-based foam and thermo plastic spacer [15].
The spacer contribute with structurally stiffness to the glazing around the edges. In this
way the glazing unit can resist deformation in response to some of the forces.
4.1.3
Sealant
The sealant structurally bonds the spacer bar together with the glass panes, while it
allow ”flexion” to accommodate glass movement [41]. Moreover it provides insulation
against moisture vapour and gas diffusion for the glazing unit.
In an IG unit, the primary sealant is applied between the spacer and the glass panes
as shown in Figure 4.3. The primary sealant helps reduce water vapour and gas per-
meability in the ”edge-of-glass area. In some cases due to reduction in strength of the
primary sealant, a secondary sealant is required to withstand the stresses caused by
temperature differences. The secondary sealant is ”placed” around the perimeter of the
glass. It has the purpose of gluing the glass panes and spacer bar together as an united
unit while preventing excessive movement under environmental stresses, such as temper-
ature [41] [40].
Figure 4.3: Single-sealed IG unit [left] and dual-sealed IG unit [right] [41].
31
==PAGE:52==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
The materials used for the primary sealant is typically synthetic rubber, like poly-
isobutylene (PIB). The materials used for the secondary sealant are different, they in-
clude polyurethane (PU), silicone (Si) and polysulfide (PS) [40].
The thickness of a primary sealant is small, around 0.2-0.6 mm depending on the
material. The secondary sealant is thicker to make sure that the primary sealant is
protected from moisture. Continuous exposure to water will make the sealant loose its
ability to adhere, which in worst case would lead to failure of the IG unit [41].
The sealants acts as the medium transferring forces between the panes and the spac-
ers. In a dual-sealed IG unit, the shear stiffness of the secondary sealant is dominating
the shear transfer between the glass panes at the edges. If the shear stiffness of the
sealant is very small, there will only be shear deformation and no shear transfer. This
would result in the glass panes deforming separately along the edges.
4.1.4
Desiccant
Desiccant is used to prevent the inside of the glass surfaces from fogging due to conden-
sation of moisture vapour or organic vapour from the interpane space [41]. It can be
integrated in the edge seal or used as a fill in the perforated hollow spacer bars, which
allow contact between the desiccant fill and the vapour [40]. Although the use of desic-
cant can help prolong the service life of a window, its capacity is limited to when it is
saturated and no longer capable of preventing fog on the internal surface of the glazing.
4.2
Structural mechanics
The window design and the components are significant to the durability of the window.
The durability is in return dependent on the structural properties of the window and also
the previously mentioned components. It is assumed that the load from the building itself
is carried by the other constructions, which imply that the main sources of mechanical
stress would be caused by wind, ambient conditions and/or thermally in the window.
The three main causes of stress in the glazing unit is concentrated in the glass panes
and the cavity of the glazing unit. It is described by the ideal gas law, expressed as in
Equation 4.1.
pV = nRT
(4.1)
Where pressure, p and Volume, V is a product of the absolute temperature,T, number
of gas molecules, n and the gas constant, R. The relation between the pressure and
volume is highly influential to the structural state of the glazing unit. The gas cavity
is described by Boyle’s law. ”The absolute pressure exerted by a given mass of an ideal
gas is inversely proportional to the volume it occupies if the temperature and amount of
32
==PAGE:53==
4.2 Structural mechanics
gas remain unchanged within a closed system”. The gas law applies for the cavity in a
glazing unit, Figure 4.4 illustrates the relation between the cavity pressure and volume.
Figure 4.4: Relation between the cavity volume and the cavity pressure [29].
The thermal insulation of the glazing is dependent on the thickness of cavity and
the cavity material. It is of that reason, an attractive idea to increase the width of
the cavity to thereby improve the thermal insulation. However from a structural sense,
the main problem would be the decrease in the balance of load sharing between the
glass panes. This would in return increase the displacement of load-carrying glass pane,
whereby the displacement of the indirectly load-carrying pane is decreased. An increase
in cavity thickness would also extend the displacement caused by changes in ambient
pressure or temperatures. A good illustration of this is presented by figure Figure 4.5,
indicating that role of cavity volume for load sharing and thermal expansion. The loads
are more evenly distributed by smaller cavity volume and the panes would in case of
thermal expansion, ”bulge” less than glazing units with larger cavities.
Figure 4.5: Pressure change as a function to a change in volume [29].
33
==PAGE:54==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
4.2.1
Stress situations
According to the ideal gas law, the cavity pressure will be increased with an increase
of temperature, this would in return cause the glass panes of the glazing to ”bulge
outwards” at both sides of the glzing (A). If the temperature decreases, the opposite
effect would occur thereby causing the glass panes to ”bulge inwards” (b), see Figure 4.6.
When the glazing is exposed to solar radiation, some of the heat is absorbed by
the glass whereas the reminder is reflected or transmitted. The absorbed energy will
increase the temperature of the glass. The warmer areas expand relative to the cooler
areas which then generates a tensile stress in the cooler regions of the glass. If the tem-
perature difference is high enough, the stress would in the end cause the glass to break
[18].
Changes in the ambient pressure have similar influence to the glazing unit. In case of
an increase of ambient pressure, a differential pressure between the lower cavity pressure
and higher ambient pressure would then cause the glass panes to ”bulge inwards” on
both sides. A decrease in ambient pressure would on the other hand cause the glass
panes to ”bulge outwards”.
In cases of wind, the cavity pressure will cause the wind load to be shared by all the
panes in the glazing. This would result in all panes ”bulging inwards” in one direction
(C), as presented in Figure 4.6. The thicknesses of the glass panes and cavities are the
deciding factors of the load sharing between the panes, as it is not necessarily equal as
aforementioned.
Figure 4.6: Deformation of IG unit [29].
34
==PAGE:55==
4.3 Assembly of glazing & wall design
4.2.2
Structural concerns
It is therefore of importance to address the cavities with regards to both thermal and
structural influences. The load sharing ability is dependent on the volume and pressure
and not the type of gas filling. The gas filling or air is only relevant for thermal purposes
and show no differences in thermal expansion.
The same thing concerns the spacer bar and sealant in case of a sealed glazing. Dur-
ing deformation caused by the situations shown in Figure 4.6, the spacer bar and sealant
(in case of sealed glazing) experiences compression and tensile forces. When the glazing
bulge inwards, the spacer is compressed by compression forces and the sealant stretched
by the the tensile forces. In case of outwards bulging, the opposite would occur as the
spacer is stretched and the sealant is compressed.
The materials used for the window components have to both be low conducting but
also have a reasonable strength. This involves the material’s compression and tensile
strength, and the stiffness. The main concern of the project is the thermal and opti-
cal part of the glazing units and not the structural. Nevertheless, the strength of the
materials are fundamental to make sure the creation of the windows is possible. The
validation of the structural capacities is based on confirmation from manufactures, other
studies or comparison to conventional cases.
4.3
Assembly of glazing & wall design
Both the default window construction together with the default wall construction was
sketched and drawn in AutoCAD. The final CAD drawings were modelled in the mul-
tiphysics program Comsol, which conducts a finite element (FE) analysis to thereafter
solve it. A detailed model of the windows and the construction parts was created with
the properties of the materials and its respective boundary conditions.
4.3.1
Glazing units
The two window concepts are similar in the sense that both have no conventional window
frame. Both concepts are created to reduce or permanently remove the thermal bridge
caused by the connection between the wall and the window. The difference is found
in the edge seal, one with sealant and the other one without sealant. All the window
components addressed in section 4.1 were created in the models. The windows were
drawn and designed in accordance to avoid the potential risks and problems that follow
with each of the components. The two figures shown below illustrates how the glazing
units could look in a cross section. Although some differences in design and structure
of the glazing might occur.
35
==PAGE:56==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
4.3.1.1
Frameless sealed quadruple glazing
The default concept is based on that from MEM4WIN’s project. Two ideas were consid-
ered, an open-able option by sliding the window to the side. The other one is a sealed
and fixed quadruple window. The default properties of the quadruple windows were
glass of 4 mm at the front and back of the glazing, with a pair of 2 mm glass in between.
The cavities are 22 mm thick with with 90% argon and 10% dry air.
The default horizontal cross section of a sealed glazing unit is presented in Figure 4.7.
The outer panes in both direction are thicker (4 mm), the spacer profile design was in-
spired by Swisspacer. The green part below the spacer resembles the sealant, function
to keep the unit together.
Figure 4.7: Sealed glazing units with coloured components.
4.3.1.2
Non-sealed multi-pane glazing
The non-sealed glazing is only sealed outside the glazing structure to keep the unit to-
gether and connect to the wall construction. The two examples in Figure 4.8 show a
concept with spacer profile sealed at the bottom (left). The sealant could seal the glaz-
ing unit together with glazing block which in return is incorporated to the structural
part of the wall. The other example to the right, show glass panes incorporated into
a wall-window structure which could easily be implemented as a part of the wall. The
glass panes are inserted into to the attachment without any sealant as the cavities will
be of dry air.
The main focus of the project will be on the sealed units. The non-sealed glazing
should be investigated in future studies and designed and analysed more thoroughly as
the concept is completely new and not present in any ways other than ideas and slightly
covered in ”Durability of future energy-efficient building components” by D. Lauritsen
[26].
36
==PAGE:57==
4.3 Assembly of glazing & wall design
Figure 4.8: Non-sealed glazing unit, coloured according to components.
4.3.2
Wall design
The wall construction becomes crucial to the properties of the window, as the frame is
replaced by direct implementation of the windows to the wall. The structural, thermal
and serviceable parts of the structure, all constitute an connecting bond to create a de-
sired window. Different wall constructions for a modern residential building in Denmark
were considered. The main structural part of the building was composed of a prefabri-
cated wooden structure of 45x45 mm wooden beams and 15 mm thick plywood plates
as shown in Figure 4.9. The length between the columns measures 1.2 m and the empty
space in the middle represents the space to incorporate the window.
Figure 4.9: Front view of wooden structure cross section in wall.
37
==PAGE:58==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
In Figure 4.10 and Figure 4.11 the horizontal wooden columns are shown. Those
columns lies atop the vertical sectioned wooden columns. On the inside of the columns
lies a structural layer, which for this case was defined as plywood.
This part is in
contact with the structural wall made of a material with strong structural properties.
This structural design constitutes the wooden skeleton and is a prefabricated structural
construction in a typical light-weight buildings. Small variations to horizontal wall lay-
ers and the materials used in the wall was considered in terms of incorporation to the
wall. The different window concepts were also influential to the design of the wall design.
Figure 4.10: Vertical cross section in top side of wall-window.
The vertical cross section of the wall-window is presented in Figure 4.10 for the top
section and in Figure 4.11 for the bottom section. The main difference between the two
figures was the outer wall layer. The bottom section has an thin flashing covering the
outer wall layer between each window. The flashing is purposely angled to accommodate
the removal of pouring rain from the window and wall. A point connection is made at
the edge of the window and the interior wall plate for both top and bottom. Another
point connection is also made one the other side, which at the bottom involves fasten
the flashing together with it and to the structural plywood.
38
==PAGE:59==
4.3 Assembly of glazing & wall design
Figure 4.11: Vertical cross section in bottom side of wall-window.
4.3.3
Mounting & attachments
The conventional process of mounting it to first mount the glazing block under the
glazing unit to the window frame, which in return is mounted to the wall. As the win-
dow frame is replaced with a prefabricated wall designed to fit a frameless window, the
mounting procedure works differently. The glazing is attached together to one unit and
thereby directly attached to the wall construction. Sealant tape is placed around the
glazing units, at the sides, top and bottom. The primary function of the sealant tape is
to prevent leakages from inside a building around the glazing, while protecting vulnera-
ble parts of the glazing.
The idea was now to incorporate the glazing directly to the wall. The main concern
of the mounting process was to connect the glazing unit together with the wall without
damaging the glass while function as a structural barrier either capable of transporting
forces or carry the load itself without damaging the glazing itself. The following parts
describes the function of the glazing packers, the material used to attach the glazing
unit.
4.3.3.1
Use of Glazing packers
The process of mounting the glazing units to the window frame normally involves at-
taching the edge of the glass to glazing packers, which in this case connects the wall
together with the glazing unit while avoiding directly contact between the edge of the
glass and the wall [39]. In this case the packers help carry the weight of the glazing unit
in the bottom. It is important that the packers are at least as wide as the thickness
of the glazing unit to cover the whole unit. The window frame was therefore replaced
with the wall which now functions as window frame construction. The benefits of using
39
==PAGE:60==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
glazing packers include the increase of window security around fixing points to prevent
deflection of the frame extrusion and the packers are designed to work with Bridge pack-
ers to maintain drainage and ventilation in the window frame rebate1. Figure 4.12 show
an illustration of how the glazing unit is attached to a glazing packer, in the example
the product Universal Packer from SEAC is used.
Figure 4.12: Attachment of glazing unit to glazing packer [39].
The use of glazing packers provides following functions as prescribed in SEAC glazing
guide.
• Load support
• Ease of movement of the leaf
• Securing of a circumferential vapour pressure compensation
• Protection of the edge compound of the insulating glass
• Prevents contact between glass and frame (wall)
• Prevention of glass slipping
• Relieving the glass edge
There exist several types of packers with different sets of functions. ”Support packer”
help conduct the weight of the glazing unit to the frame. ”Bridge packers” ensure a
circumferential vapour pressure compensation on a flat base of the rebate. Whereas
compensating blocks ensure flat support and form the base for the glazing packers. Two
examples of glazing packers from SEAC is presented in Figure 4.13 and Figure 4.14,
both have functions similar as aforementioned above.
1Rebate - Part of the frame or glazing bar that receives the glass
