==PAGE:61==
4.3 Assembly of glazing & wall design
Figure 4.13: SEAC
universal
glazing
packer [39].
Figure 4.14: SEAC
standard
glazing
packer [39].
4.3.3.2
General mounting considerations
Designing the attachment of the frameless windows in the wall construction involves
the replacement of the frame structure with a direct incorporation to the wall. Some
considerations had to be taken with regards to both structure, repair and thermal prepa-
rations. All information was based on that found from Eckelt Guidelines [16] and/or
SEAC guidelines [39].
It was emphasised that the packers were placed correctly to accommodate its function
and avoid breakage. Glasindustrien created a guideline in cooperation with Teknologisk
Institut (2011) for mounting different glazing in the wall construction [17]. The guideline
addresses the concern for the structural capabilities and the risks that follow. The guide
mentions the importance of using the right type of attachments to integrate the glaz-
ing correctly. The guide specifically mentioned the three fundamental types of glazing
blocks, ranged from 1 to 3 as shown in Figure 4.15.
Setting block (1) - Is used for all types of glazing to carry the loads between the
glazing and frame cavity, which constitute the stability of the the whole construction.
Distance block (2) - Is not used for fixed windows but for e.g sliding windows. The
primary function of the Distance block is to ensure the distance between glazing edge
and the frame cavity. It is only necessary for glazing with a width larger than 500 mm.
Support block (3) - Not essential but used to ensure the attachments are function-
ally and for the centring of the glazing during transport and use during mounting.
41
==PAGE:62==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
Figure 4.15: Examples of different ways to install windows [17].
The mounting of the windows and the specific functions required is presented below
with following:
• Structural load capacity, to carry the weight of the glazing
• Fixation of unit to withstand wind and tensile forces
• Air, water and vapour tightness
• Covering of glazing unit to outer facade
• Encapsulation of bottom part, sides and top part of the unit.
• Gas-tight in case of sealed glazing, to keep gas cavities intact
Furthermore, it was stated by the guideline provided by Glasindustrien that packers
and blocks are required to have the same thickness as the glazing unit. Fixed windows
with a width larger than 1800 mm requires to the blocks to be placed from the middle
of the block to the edge by half of the total width of the glazing.
4.4
Detailed wall-window considerations
The presented content introduced the different glazing design and components to create
a window by integrating the glazing unit together with the wall construction. Three
different designs, involving a fixed sealed window with ventilation hatch, a sliding win-
dow, partly fixed and partly openable and an improved window frame construction for
quadruple glazing. In this part, the detailed designs and structure of those are explained.
4.4.1
Fixed sealed window in wall
The sealed window is placed in the wall by fixing it with the blocks directly to the wall
instead of a frame. This would require it to integrate to a strong structural part of
the wall. With the demonstrated wall design this should be possible. The edge-of-glass
42
==PAGE:63==
4.4 Detailed wall-window considerations
is placed in glazing packer, which is the support packer. Below the packer, a block is
placed to fix the window to the wall construction. This would help carry some of the
load in case of wind. All glass panes must be covered by the glazing blocks from below.
First example is presented in Figure 4.16. The glazing unit is single sealed with a
glazing block below the glazing connecting the glazing to the structural part of the wall.
The corners around the unit is sealed with sealant and gasket (EPDM) to provide a soft
contact between the bottom surface of the glass panes and the wall. The sealant helps
keep the window fixed.
Figure 4.16: Mounting of fixed sealed window.
4.4.2
Fixed non-sealed window
The glass panes of the non-sealed window are fixed to a glazing packers with additional
features, acting like a spacer profile but directly integrated to the wall with a glazing
block beneath. The darker spot at the top are EPDM rubber to create soft contact
between the glass panes and the wall. The space below and in between the EPDM and
the wall-window construction is sealed off with polysulfide sealant to protect the glazing
from water, air and moisture. Sealant could be implemented in other smaller parts but
is too small to be considered in Figure 4.17. The remaining parts of the window is
non-sealed and more directly integrated to the wall.
43
==PAGE:64==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
Figure 4.17: Mounting of fixed non-sealed window.
4.4.3
Sliding window structure
A fixed window eliminates the possibility of providing ventilation, which for many is a
necessity. The sliding window introduces a completely new concept of constructing a
slidable window. The idea was to create a frameless window with an openable element,
as the main role of a window is to provide daylight and ventilation for the occupants.
The whole window construction is created without any frame structure and consist
of two parts. One part is the slidable window, big enough to provide enough ventilation
without causing draught. The second part is fixed to the wall and not openable and
provides the majority of the daylight intake. The window utilise the structural elements
of the wall to provide static stability to the unit. The window was designed in both
horizontal and vertical cross section to give a full picture of the design and function.
4.4.3.1
Horizontal cross section
Figure 4.18 illustrates the horizontal cross section plan overview of the window-wall
construction with the sliding window. The sliding part is on the left side, slightly in-
corporated into the structural plywood and to a magnet folder with the surrounding
magnetic seal. The idea was to make sure that the window is fixed in contact with the
structural elements and sealed tightly with the magnet when closed. The fixed window
can be seen on the right side of Figure 4.18, with one part connecting it together with
the sliding window. The other part (left) of the window is incorporated into the wall
44
==PAGE:65==
4.4 Detailed wall-window considerations
similarly as the sliding window but without the magnet application. Some vertical heat
losses are present around the wall-window connection, those were investigated in later
parts of the project.
The glazing is identical to the other concepts with a quadruple glazing. The main
attribute about this concept is that the frameless window is not entirely fixed and still
partly open-able. One part of the window is fixed as the sealed window concept, mounted
and sealed in the same way. The other part comprise of a smaller glazing unit sealed
together with magnetic seal and a magnet connection where they connect. The open-
able sliding window is smaller than the fixed window but big enough to provide enough
ventilation. The ”magnetic seal” in the connection glazing is mostly made up of elastic
rubber with tiny fractions of aluminium, with a small air cavity in the middle of the
connection. A 2 mm thin Glass Fibre Reinforced Polyester (GFRP) profile was imple-
mented around the edges on both windows and to seal off at the part connecting the
two windows. The principle was to apply a magnet on the magnetic seal in the magnet
connection, at the point it ”seals” the intersection between the fixed window and the
sliding window. A small magnet is applied on the magnetic seal for both windows.
Figure 4.18: Horizontal cross section of sliding window in wall construction.
45
==PAGE:66==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
The window’s opening/closing function is based on the principle of a modern fridge.
A handle connected to the window construction is proposed to be part of the sliding
window as shown in Figure 4.19. The process of drawing the handle is similar to that
with a lever. The handle is divided into two parts pivoted at a fixed point which becomes
the centre of rotation. The longer part of the handle is the one drawn by the occupant.
The other part is fixed and the force applied becomes larger at the smaller part making
it possible to easily ”push” the window or ”drag” it to close.
Figure 4.19: Horizontal cross section of lever, showing lever of sliding window .
The circle in Figure 4.18 illustrates the detailed structure of the magnet connection,
connecting the fixed and sliding window. Figure 4.21 show the opening and Figure 4.20
the closing of the connection. The GFRP profile on the outside of both windows, function
as structure to keep the glazing and sealant tape with magnet intact. The GFRP profile
on the windows do not meet which meant small air cavities on parts of the construction.
The small openings were purposely made to avoid contact between the glass panes and
the profile during opening or closing of the sliding window, to avoid scratching of the
glass panes.
The sealant tape was made of flexible rubber with small fragments of
aluminium. This helped the sealant tape to have a small magnet (thickness ∼1 mm)
connecting to the magnet on the extended sealant tape of the fixed window part.
46
==PAGE:67==
4.4 Detailed wall-window considerations
Figure 4.20: Closed magnet connection.
Figure 4.21: Open magnet connection.
Another design was created to minimise the thermal bridges in the magnet connection.
As it was quite obvious that the magnet connection would be highly exposed to outdoor
conditions. High heat losses would occur around the connection due to the high ther-
mal conductivity of the materials and lack of thermal insulation. Another problem was
the exposure to outside vapour and moisture which could lead to condensation problems.
The solution was to create a bigger window construction by increasing the space
between the glazing profile and the GFRP profile. The air cavity in both windows were
replaced with Aerogel insulation, which is a lightweight (ρm = 20 kg/m3) insulation
material with a very low thermal conductivity. About 20 mm of Aerogel was placed in
the spacer between the glazing and the GFRP profile.
Figure 4.22: Detailed cross cross section of magnet connection with Aerogel insulation
.
47
==PAGE:68==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
The opening and closing of the windows is the same as in the case presented in
Figure 4.20 and Figure 4.21. The full structure show a larger intersection but remains
otherwise similar to the original model.
Figure 4.23: Horizontal plan cross section of sliding window with Aerogel insulation
in wall construction.
4.4.3.2
Vertical cross section
The vertical cross section of the window to wall construction of the sliding concept is
illustrated in Figure 4.24. The figure shows the top construction, which was modified
to better fit the window and insulate the sliding element. The position of the beams
and the wooden plate was switched to accommodate more space to place the sliding
part. This would help hide it for aesthetically reasons but also help to reduce heat losses
around the element. The sliding window is incorporated through the ”sliding element”
which is made of structural Steel and with a sphere also of steel, helping to slide the
window to both open and close it. As aforementioned, a GFRP profile was created to
give the glazing unit mechanical protection. The GFRP profile is glued to the vertical
sides of the glazing to mechanically connect the sliding element together with the win-
dow by the GFRP profile. This would make the sliding element carry the sliding part
of the window in both ends by transferring the static forces through the construction.
48
==PAGE:69==
4.4 Detailed wall-window considerations
The whole construction was incorporated into the structural part of the wall by
drilling a bolt from the sliding element into to the I-profile. The element can carry
weight larger than 200 kg, which was suitable for the glazing, as the weight of a quadru-
ple glazing with 4 mm glass panes reaches about 40 kg/m2. The two windows were
separated by a thick layer of sealant tape with the magnet in between. Insulation was
placed in the gaps between the sliding element and the beams, to insulate exposed parts
of the construction.
Figure 4.24: Vertical cross section of sliding window in wall.
The sliding window was placed in front of the fixed window due to the risk of draining
rain and other outside conditions which could affect the building interior. One example
would be the limitation of the space needed for a window sill to angle the outside wall
layer and thereby help drain the gathered rain away from the wall to the ground. As
seen from Figure 4.24, an additional beam was placed next to the sliding element to
help attach the fixed window to the structural part of the wall as the wooden plate was
placed further away from its reach.
The bottom construction of the horizontal cross section was also designed, with three
examples illustrating how the construction could be build to accommodate the angled
flashing and how the two windows could be attached to the wall, side by side.
49
==PAGE:70==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
First example was to place a flashing of galvanised steel and pinpoint it together with
the point connection and place it slightly above the wall construction and leave some
space. The sliding window had to be placed slightly above the fixed window.
Figure 4.25: Vertical cross section of bottom wall-window, example 1.
In the second example, some aesthetic adjustments were made to level the two win-
dows side by side and avoid differences in view through both windows. The wall con-
struction was changed. The wooden column was displaced to the inside to make place
for fixing the flashing together with the point connection. This was done with a steel
nail going through the wooden beam, which would fixate the bottom structure. It also
meant a reduction in height at the bottom edge of the wall to then have the flashing.
The insulation was protected by placing an additional layer of plywood atop, connecting
to the beam. The two windows were connected through the sealant tape as seen from
the vertical cross section.
50
==PAGE:71==
4.4 Detailed wall-window considerations
Figure 4.26: Vertical cross section of bottom wall-window, example 2.
A third example similar to the second example was made with a different design to
the flashing. The purpose was to accommodate space for insulation between the sliding
glazing part of the window and the flashing. As the glazing was exposed without any
real insulation, an 20 mm thick layer of Aerogel was placed in between the GFRP profile
and the bottom part of the glazing, to fill out the air cavity with Aerogel insulation.
This would help reduce the heat losses from the glazing.
Another obstacle was the large open cavity between the modified glazing bottom and
the window flashing. This meant that the bottom of the glazing and the outer part of
the flashing was still heavily exposed to outdoor conditions. This was solved by placing
an insulation block slightly below the GFRP profile with a distance of 1 mm separating
them and fixating the block to the flashing, thereby covering the outer part of it. This
would significantly help reduce heat losses around the construction as the open cavity
of air would be replaced with low conducting insulation. If the thermal bridge of the
bottom sliding window is far too high for the two other examples, the third example
would be the ideal design choice.
51
==PAGE:72==
4 Mechanics & assembly of glazing
Figure 4.27: Vertical cross section of bottom wall-window, example 3.
4.4.4
Integrated frameless window-ventilation hatch
The ventilation hatch was created in cooperation with a fellow student Elizabeth Saliba
and supervisor prof. Svend Svendsen. The detailed work can be found in ”Design of cost
effective Nearly Zero Energy Buildings focusing on new types of windows”.
The sealed fixed window model was created with the addition of a ventilation hatch,
specifically designed to accommodate sufficient natural ventilation. The glazing unit
and ventilation hatch was designed as an integration of the two products. This would
provide an aesthetic design with the possibility of buying a complete product including
both a window and a ventilation hatch and thereby also ease the incorporation to the
walls.
The ventilation hatch has the primary function of providing sufficient ventilation when
open as the window was fixed. The ventilation hatch was designed to include several
other functions. The outer part includes louvre grill, composed of air and in the cavity
includes sound absorbers to provide acoustical protection from outside noise.
Other
functions include filtering of bugs and dirt while providing some thermal insulation, to
reduce heat losses and vapour flows from outside. The hatch was also designed to both
be theft-proof and provide a fire escape.
52
==PAGE:73==
4.4 Detailed wall-window considerations
A detailed horizontal cross section of the hatch is shown in Figure 4.28. The ventila-
tion hatch was divided into three boxes, the outer box composed of a aluminium louvre
grill with aluminium shaped sound absorbers. The middle part is presented as an empty
air gap but this part would include bug-filter. The inner part of the hatch was created
from Vacuum Insulated Panel (VIP) which measures 50 mm. The panels were covered
with 2 mm GFRP profile to keep the panels intact and avoid puncture or any other
damage. Figure 4.29 illustrates the horizontal cross section of the hatch.
Figure 4.28: Vertical cross section of ventilation hatch and glazing.
Figure 4.29: Horizontal cross section of ventilation hatch and glazing.

the glazing unit with a Magnet Gasket. A blade hinge integrated between the wall and
one corner of the hatch, this would make it possible for the hatch to open inwards to the
room. The part of the hatch connected to the glazing unit with a magnet connection was
similar to that described for the sliding window. The main difference was the direction
of opening.
Figure 4.30: Horizontal cross section of ventilation hatch and glazing.
The opening of the magnet connection is based on the principle illustrated in Fig-
ure 4.31. The magnetic strip of the sealant tape connects the magnet on the GFRP
profile of the glazing unit together with the magnetic strip on the ventilation hatch.
The magnet connection has an air filled gasket which is in its ”resting position” when
closed. A handle is drawn similar to that of a modern fridge. The force applied to open
the hatch result in the pressurisation of the air filled gasket as shown in Figure 4.31.
This helps the magnet on the GFRP profile to release the magnetic strip on the hatch to
thereby open. The louvre grill or aluminium frame is always closed, which emphasis that
only the GFRP framed Vacuum Insulated Panel (VIP) is opened to provide ventilation.
The Aluminium frame is fixed and theft-proof but can be dismounted from the inside.

4.4.5
Reference window, quadruple glazing
The current window market is dominated by window frame concept. The frame materials
are usually made of either wood, metal or combination of those and in more recent time,
reinforced plastic. The main problem with a window frame are the large heat losses
caused by the frame itself and from between the glazing and frame and between the
window and wall construction. Significant efforts have been made to reduce the heat
losses, one example is the construction of a GFRP window frame for a triple glazing. It
involved the creation of such frame with minimal use of the frame material and adding
insulation and rubber. The window frame design was an attempt to reduce the size and
thereby heat losses from the window frame. The GFRP window frame was created for
a triple glazing and later modified to fit a quadruple glazing in Prof. Svendsen course
”Sustainable Buildings”. It was assumed that extending the width of the frame would be
enough to accommodate an additional glass pane without causing any structural failures.
The proposed reference window with GFRP frame is defined as a pivot window, which
emphasise that the window opens inwards from the top and remain closed in the bottom.
This window model is considered the reference model.
56
==PAGE:77==
4.4 Detailed wall-window considerations
4.4.5.1
Horizontal cross section of bottom model
The bottom unit was created by attaching the window frame to the structural wall
plate with foam rubber and inserting some insulation in the cavity between. A point
connection was placed next to the window frame, attaching the whole unit together with
the wall as seen from Figure 5.40 and fasten the flashing to the wall.
Figure 4.34: Horizontal cross section of bottom reference wall-window.
4.4.5.2
Horizontal cross section of top model
Similar to the bottom structure with small differences to the wall-window frame connec-
tion, the same principle of mounting exist. The window sill is replaced with outer wall
plate sealing the exterior part of the wall to the window frame.

The reference model is a pivot window which means that the window is opened by tilting
it inwards with a opening either in the top and closed in the bottom. This will allow
airflow in and out while remain theft-proof and difficult to open from outside.

CHAPTER 5
Heat transfer of analysis of
windows
The different window designs presented in previous chapter was modelled in COMSOL
Multiphysics 5.3 for heat transfer evaluation. The aim was to find the temperatures and
linear losses in the different window designs.
5.1
Modelling with COMSOL
The numerical investigations of the window designs and its thermal properties was con-
ducted in COMSOL. For each investigation a simple model resembling the considered
model was created by drawing in AutuCAD and inserted into the program. COMSOL
is a Finite Element (FE) analysis, solver and multiphysics simulation software program.
What makes COMSOL unique is the option to combine multiple physical fields and solve
these by the use of the Finite Element Method (FEM). The basic thing is that a mesh
is created for a geometry resembling a model. Every mesh becomes smaller entities
containing a number of Partial Differential Equations (PDE) describing the property
of the entity, which for this project was a combination of heat transfer in solids and
fluids. COMSOL helps solve this by gathering all created elements and give an flexible
and accurate solution to the given problem. This make COMSOL flexible, accurate and
robust. It was the most optimal software program for the numerical simulations for
those reasons.
5.1.1
Basics of Heat Transfer module
The window components and the materials of such were defined accordingly with COM-
SOL’s heat transfer module. The calculations of heat transfer in COMSOL is based on
that of heat flux according to Fourier’s law. The heat flux is expressed as Equation 5.1,
with dependency on the thermal conductivity of the material and the temperature gra-
dient, ∇T.
q = −k · ∇T
(5.1)
==PAGE:82==
5 Heat transfer of analysis of windows
COMSOL requires the density (ρm), constant heat capacity (Cp) and thermal conduc-
tivity (λ) of a material to simulate the heat transfer of a model. Although it is primarily
the thermal conductivity that is used. In cases when the other two parameters for a
material were unknown, an assumption was made without influencing the final results.
The materials and properties of those were found by researching product catalogues and
studies conducted on the thermal properties of those. Not all values were completely
certain but was close enough to provide an acceptable estimate.
5.2
Modelling of thermal linear losses in
wall-window connection
The prescribed method is identical for all cases of both framless windows and the Refer-
ence window. This example follows the two-box spacer profile model, which assumes that
the spacer profile and sealant are two boxes in the computationally model. The upper
box is the spacer and the lower box is sealant, with a typical thickness of 3 mm. The
method also applies for other modelling of spacer profiles. For the other cases the method-
ology and approach is similar but with some variations to material definitions and design.
The heat loss through the connection between the window and the wall construction
is the desired value. This value is also known as the linear thermal transmittance or the
linear loss (Ψconnection). For a window-wall construction, the two main heat losses are
found in the glazing (Ug), the horizontal direction of the wall (Uwall) and the thermal
bridges of the construction. All these constitutes the total heat loss through the whole
model (Φtot). By subtracting the losses of the glazing and the wall, the remaining heat
loss can be said to be the linear loss experienced at the wall-window connection as
shown in Equation 5.2. The heat loss is the same as the heat transmission and the
symbol ”Phi (Φ)” was therefore used. The glazing-wall construction was divided into
one part representing the visible part of the glazing (0.19 m) and 1.22 m wall without
the glazing.
Ψconnection = Φtot −Φg −Φwall
∆T
= Φtot −Ug · lg −Uwall · lwall
(5.2)
The numerical investigation was done according to ISO standard 10077-2:2017. This
meant that some precautions were made when designing the numerical models. The wall
model was created for a typical horizontal cross section per 1.2 m of the wall, to include
the horizontal layers, structural components as beams, columns and wall plates. All
necessary details were included and those deemed unnecessary were either simplified or
removed from the model. The heat losses through the different parts were simplified to
be one-dimensional, in one direction so to speak. An illustrative example of the scenario
of heat losses is generalised in Figure 5.1.
62
==PAGE:83==
5.2 Modelling of thermal linear losses in wall-window connection
Figure 5.1: Heat losses through window-wall construction according to ISO 10211.
The investigated window wall construction was sectioned horizontally. To simplify
the calculations, the interior temperature (Ti) was defined as 1◦C and the exterior tem-
perature (Te) was defined as 0◦C. This would give a temperature difference (∆T) of
1K, when calculating the thermal bridge. The boundary lines of the model were defined
with an exterior resistance of 0.04 m2K/W, interior resistances of 0.125 m2K/W and 0.2
m2K/W for the interior corner part between window and upper wall layer (max 30 mm
both direction). An illustration of this is presented in Figure 5.2.
Figure 5.2: Detailed model of window/wall construction in COMSOL.
63
==PAGE:84==
5 Heat transfer of analysis of windows
The U-value of the glazing unit was found by calculation according to ISO stan-
dard 15099 [20]. However, to simplify matters, Pilkington spectrum or Calcumen by
Saint-Gobain was used to calculate it. For more specific cases involving complex layers
and/or multipanes, the glazing properties were found with Window 7.6 by Berkeley Lab.
The small geometry of the glazing was compensated for by applying the equivalent
thermal conductivity (λeff) of the window cavity in between the glass panes.
This
would help simulate the heat losses through the glazing more accurately. The thermal
conductivity of the window cavity was calculated by dividing the length of the cavity
(Lcav) with the resistance of the cavity as shown in Equation 5.3.
λeff =
Lcav
1
Ug −Rse −Rsi
(5.3)
The U-value of the wall was found by addressing the in-homogeneous wall horizon-
tally by layer, thickness and thermal conductivity of the layer material. In this way the
thermal resistance (R) was calculated as follows.
R = d
λ
(5.4)
U =
1
∑R
(5.5)
The sealed fixed window models have an un-ventilated cavity below the spacer bar.
In some other cases slightly ventilated air cavities could occur dependent on the model.
The calculations of the thermal conductivity of the cavities were based on those from
ISO standard 10077-2:2017 [19].
The entire construction simplified for simulation as in COMSOL in Figure 5.3 was
used to calculate the total heat transmission. The part below the detailed cross section
of the wall construction was extended to a length of 1.2 m. To create a model with
full lengths as a real construction with 1:1 dimensions. It was also a way of creating
a model with the real number of columns and beams in the structure. The remaining
parts were kept with the same dimensions. The three parts were drawn and defined
with its respective properties. As aforementioned, COMSOL conducts a Finite Element
(FE) analysis of the figures, by ”meshing” the figures into small tetrahedrons. All parts
were analysed and the heat distribution was calculated in every single part, providing a
detailed and accurate temperature distribution in the models. This made it possible to
calculate the heat transmission and temperature at any point in the model.
Figure 5.3 show the lengths of the glazing and wall used in the modelling and cal-
culations. The figure represents a typical cross section of the window-wall construction.
The dividing line separating the dimensions is at the point where the transparent or
visible part of the glazing unit starts. The visible glazing length measures 0.19 m and
the total length of the wall measures 1.22 m, which in return result in a total model
length of 1.41 m.

==PAGE:85==
5.2 Modelling of thermal linear losses in wall-window connection
Figure 5.3: Detailed model of window/wall construction in COMSOL.

re-calculated in COMSOL to see if the principle of defining the equivalent thermal
conductivity in a glazing would provide a similar U-value.
The U-value of the wall
was found by conducting a COMSOL modelling of a typical cross section of the wall
without window. The U-value of the wall was calculated by division with length used
in the wall model. The heat transmission used for the thermal bridge calculations was

5 Heat transfer of analysis of windows
5.2.1
Example to calculation of thermal bridges
The following parts use the defined dimensions and values to illustrate the general mod-
elling and calculation to find the thermal linear transmittance (thermal bridge) of the
window-wall construction.
The full model with a length of 1.41 m including both window and wall was modelled
to find the heat transmission through the model. The temperature distribution of the
construction was as presented in Figure 5.4. The figure illustrates how the largest tem-
perature division is centred around the window connection as the temperature becomes
homogeneous on the ”interior” part of the construction due to low conducting materials
and with increasing thickness. The transmission through the interior boundary lines
was 0.23481 W/m for this scenario.
Figure 5.4: Temperature distribution of window-wall construction.
The wall model was constructed with the load carrying part of the wall drawn in the
middle of the horizontal wall layer. In that way the linear thermal transmittance around
the structural parts would be considered. The length of this geometry was likewise 1.2
m as the whole construction model. The upper layers around the glazing was included
in the calculations by adding the remaining 20 mm of the wall plate. The heat loss of
the wall model was calculated to be 0.1025 W/m. The U-value of the wall was then
0.0854 W/m2K with a wall length of 1.2 m and considering the temperature difference.
The value used for to calculate the thermal bridge was therefore the U-value multiplied
with the total wall length 1.22 m giving 0.1042 W/mK.
66
==PAGE:87==
5.2 Modelling of thermal linear losses in wall-window connection
Figure 5.5: Temperature distribution of the wall construction.
The U-value of the glazing for this example was 0.35 W/m2K, according to Pilkington
spectrum calculator. The effective thermal conductivity of the cavity was according to
Equation 5.3 approximately 0.022 W/mK for this model. This meant a heat transmission
of 0.34915 W/m in the simulation model. This value applied for 1 m, which had to be
fractioned according to the length of the glazing in the detailed window/wall model. The
length of the glazing was 0.190 m, which meant a heat transmission of 0.066 W/m.
Figure 5.6: Temperature distribution of the glazing unit.
67
==PAGE:88==
5 Heat transfer of analysis of windows
The thermal bridge of the window connection could then be calculated by subtracting
the heat transmission of the glazing and wall from the total and divide by the tempera-
ture difference, which for this case was 1K. The results in Table 5.2 show the calculated
heat transmission adjusted to the lengths in the full model with the thermal bridge of
the window-wall connection (Ψconnection).

5.2.2
Wall construction
The wall construction was designed for a residential building meeting the Danish Build-
ing Regulation 2020. The construction is a ”quasi-homogeneous” wall with different
layers. Most of the material used were conventional and has been frequently used in
buildings. However, some more different materials and implementations were made. Ex-
planation of these can be found further down in the chapter. The different construction
parts were categorised according to its respective functions. The density (ρm), thermal
conductivity (λ) and specific heat capacity (Cp) were defined for each materials.
The wall plates were used as the interior layer of the wall to cover the wall at the
edges. The conventional way is to use Wood and/or a plastic material as cover. An
alternative way was to implement a plastic encapsulated vacuum panel. The vacuum
panel from Va-Q-Tec has a significant lower thermal conductivity and could help with
a reduction to heat losses in the wall-window region.
One of the main problems is that the panel is quite vulnerable and could puncture
and loose its insulating ability. The panel has to therefore be protected by a stronger
material which is placed around as cover, normally some sort of plastic material.
Another point to make is that highly conducting materials such as aluminium is
used to seal the vulnerable vacuum material in a Vacuum Insulation panel (VIP). This
would create a thermal bridge between the protecting layer and the aluminium layer,
despite an ultra thin thickness. This had to be considered in the calculations to avoid
unrealistically good results. The International Energy Agency (IEA) investigated the
influence of metallic materials to the resulting conductivity of a VIP. A simple way of
including the resulting thermal conductivity was to use the centre value of the VIP and
find the resulting one between the outer layer (plastic cover) and the metallic sheet [43].
The resulting thermal conductivity of 0.9* W/mK was therefore used in the covering
material of a VIP, which for this project was plastic as presented in Table 5.3.
68

5.2 Modelling of thermal linear losses in wall-window connection

5.3
Spacer profile influence to Ψconnection
The first investigation was to find the the linear thermal transmittance (Ψconnection)
between the interaction of the glass edge and the wall construction. Different types of
spacer profiles based on products found from Bundesverband were investigated to find
the lowest linear loss. The investigation was done for sealed glazing units, one design
resembling the conventional spacer-sealant model and a newer, alternative solution. The
linear loss between the glazing and the wall is illustrated in Figure 5.7. The design of
and the equivalent thermal conductivity of the spacer profile and sealant used in the
glazing and around the connection was highly influential to the final outcome. The wall
plate (orange) beside the glazing to the interior was in default defined as wood.

5.3.1
Fixed sealed window
The fixed sealed window concept is divided into different types involving different spacer
designs, materials and sealants. It was assumed that the concepts were possible in terms
of static forces and load carrying. Two different designs were made, one considered the
default option (A) and an alternative more efficient design (B).
Default sealed glazing unit (A)
The default sealed glazing unit represents the traditional way of modelling a spacer
profile and sealant in a window. The spacer profile was designed as a two-box model
with a bottom and top ”spacer”. The bottom part represents the polysulfide sealant and
the upper part represents the spacer profile with or without sealant. The modelling is
simplification of the real design and uses the equivalent thermal conductivity to simplify
the geometry for the numerical investigations. The equivalent thermal conductivity of
the different spacer designs were taken from bundesverband-flachglas analysis and used
for the numerical simulations [11].
Figure 5.8: Sealed two box spacer.

thermal conductivity of the top spacer had to be below 0.3 W/mK to be considered
for use. The lowest linear thermal transmittance of the edge construction was with a
Edgetech Super Spacer Premium (E). This type requires hot melt sealant to be applica-
ble. It was assumed that silicone was used as primary sealant but butyl remained the
secondary sealant. The results are presented in Table 5.10.

Six-O-Four spacer (B)
The alternative spacer profile concept was found in K. Rick’s study from 2005, which is
spacer design with reduced secondary sealant (polysulfide) [36] [41]. The spacer design
was modified from a double-pane glazing to a quadruple glazing unit. An example of
how the spacer design could look was presented by Van Der Bergh in their state of the
art article addressing window components, shown in Figure 5.9. Butyl (blue) sealant
was placed between the glass panes and the spacer profile and the bottom parts below
the butyl sealant was sealed off with polysulfide (green). This would utilise the use of
the sealant more efficiently while providing an option with smaller heat losses due to
less use of the highly conductive polysulfide.
It was assumed that it was possible to construct a spacer design covering the whole
thickness, instead of separating the spacer profile between each cavities. This would give
an slightly different design as presented in Figure 5.10. However, the design was still
similar to the original idea but just extended. It was in addition to this, also assumed
that the spacer profile could be given the same equivalent thermal conductivity of spacer
products from Table 5.8 without changing the design.

to the default spacer designs. This could indicate that further investigation to different

5.4
Influence of mounted wall plate
Another subject of interest is the inside wall plate mounted next to the glazing horizon-
tally. The function of the wall plate is to cover the wall construction to the inside. The
wall plate is likewise placed to help fasten the placement of the glazing. It is marked
in red in Figure 5.11. The part connecting to the glazing could have an influence to
the linear thermal transmittance below the glazing. The same transmission values and
lengths from previous section was used.
Figure 5.11: Wall-window illustration .
A wooden plate was the default wall plate choice.
Other wall plates with lower
thermal conductivity were investigated to replace the wooden plate and reduce the linear
loss. Three examples were found from their respective manufacturers. The first was the
high density insulation Purenit produced by Puren. The other one was thin GFRP profile
covering an insulation plate, the whole construction was assumed to be one. The last
concept was a vacuum panel by Va-q-tec with super low thermal conductivity. It should
be noted that the influence of the thin aluminium foil and the plastic encapsulation
was considered in the modelling of the VIP. The three concepts have significantly lower
thermal conductivity compared to wood. The three cases were investigated with the Six-
O-four spacer design and Rolltech Multitech G as spacer material (BG) and compared
to the default model with a wooden plate.

5.5
Investigation of sliding window
The sliding window creates an option to both have a frameless and openable window,
thereby creating a new way of designing modern windows. The investigation was sim-
ilar to that of the spacer and wall plate influence to the thermal bridge around the
wall-window connection. The aim was to find the heat transmission around the con-
struction and use the results to find the linear thermal transmittance in all directions
and parts for later implementation in a simulation model.
The sliding element poses some challenges in terms of heat losses, as steel is used
to carry the sliding glazing element. Another thing is the attachment and fixation of
the sliding element which is also influential to the heat losses. The spacer profile design
(Six-O-Four) from the previous investigation was used to find the most energy efficient
spacer profile concept. The combi-sealed model with Rolltech Multitech G as spacer
profile was therefore also used for the sliding window. It was designed for both the fixed
and sliding window. The wall plate covering the wall construction was defined as an
plastic encapsulated vacuum panel.

Top section
The horizontal section present the thermal bridge through the wall-window connection.
The full model had to be divided into several models to fully find all heat losses. The
model was divided into one part with only the fixed window in the wall construction.
The other model was the heat transmission through the glazing but the same value of
0.3499 W/m from previous investigation was used.
Another model for heat losses through the glazing and the horizontal wall construction
was created similarly as done in previous cases. The sliding element was simplified and
the ”roller” was not included in the model as shown in Figure 5.14. This was due to the
model representing the cross section and specifying a depth in the Z-direction.
Figure 5.14: Sliding element, detailed (left) and simplified model for comsol (right).
A structural ”profile” was designed around the edges of the glazing unit to connect it
to the sliding element. The material used was Glass Fibre Reinforced Polyester (GFRP).
The material is strong and structurally capable of carrying higher loads and with a low
thermal conductivity of 0.3 W/mK [3]. The GFRP was glued to the glazing unit and
functioned to provide a connection between the sliding elemenent of the window and the
wall construction.
Two designs were created as shown in Figure 5.15 and Figure 5.16. The first idea was
to create the a GFRP profile integrated around all edges of the glazing, similarly to a
smaller frame. The other idea was to reduce the need of GFRP and utilise its functions
more thoroughly while minimising the use of it. Around the edges, the GFRP profile

5.5.2
Horizontal cross section
The linear thermal transmittance was also considered in the vertical cross section of the
sliding windows. The full vertical section of the model was divided into three parts to
find the temperature and heat distribution around the regions without interference from
the remaining model. The model was marked and given a letter for the areas with a
significant presence of thermal bridges as shown in Figure 5.25.
Part A of the model was the part where the sliding window is in contact with the wall
when closed. Part B was the middle part of the model, where the sliding window inter-
sects with the fixed window, integrated together through the sealant tape by a magnet.
The third part (C) was the connection between the fixed window connection to the wall.
The heat transmission was conducted in COMSOL by assuming an outdoor temperature
0◦C and indoor temperature of 1◦C. The indoor and outdoor thermal resistances for the
surfaces were also defined, as 0.125 W/mK and 0.04 W/mK respectively.
Figure 5.25: Vertical cross section .
The heat transmission through the wall and the glazing was also considered. The
default heat transmission value of 0.34915 W/m was used for glazing. The heat trans-
mission through the wall was 0.1016 W/m as in the spacer influence investigation, as
the horizontal cross section uses the default wall structure with two wooden beams. The
model was divided into three smaller models in COMSOL according to the respective
areas (A,B,C) to make sure that the specific areas were not heavily influenced by the
heat distribution from the other parts of the construction.

units differed in the three model, as in Table 5.19 categorised after selected area.

Vacuum was also tried out to see if the heat losses could be reduced further. In

losses was reduced even more, now reaching a value of 0.056 W/mK. Aerogel is however
a more realistic solution to Vacuum as it is more easily implemented and not in risk of
puncture like Vacuum.
5.5.3
Condensation risk of sliding window
One method to determine the risk of condensation is the use of Temperature Ratio Value
(TRV). The ratio is dependent on the interior and exterior temperature and the interior
surface temperature. The recommended TRV range is above a ratio of 0.7 (TRV > 0.7).
It can be seen from the horizontal cross section of the sliding window, the area at which
the two windows intersects could be potential areas of interior condensation due to large
heat losses. The area is marked by red, which is the interior surface of the glass of the
slinding part of the window. Both the original construction and the optimised part with
aerogel insulation were modelled. The indoor surface temperature was set at 20◦C and
the outdoor temperature at 0◦C
Figure 5.30: Critical temperature area of sliding window.
90
==PAGE:111==
5.5 Investigation of sliding window
The temperatures were found and the TRV was calculated for the two designs. The
temperature was quite low for the original model without any insulation around the
magnet connection. A TRV of 0.39 reflected a large risk for internal condensation. It
was therefore necessary to insulate the area to both avoid high linear losses but in addi-
tion the prospect of condensation on the interior surface of the sliding window.
The aerogel insulated magnet connection showed a great improvement in reducing the
heat losses. The interior surface temperature of the critical area was therefore increased
which meant a higher TRV. However the recommendations have stated that the TRV
should be higher than a ratio of 0.7.
However, it was stated by a report made by
Jørgen M.Schultz and Henrik Jørgensen in 2014 that the minimum interior temperature
of the window should be 9.3◦C according to BR10 4 [23]. The aerogel insulated magnet
connection in the sliding window was therefore considered the finalised design.

5.7
Reference models
Two window model with window frame structure were created with the intention of
comparing the best available windows to the proposal of frameless windows. The triple
98
==PAGE:119==
5.7 Reference models
glazing has been available on the market for a while, replacing the double insulated glaz-
ing unit. Alternative window frames made of material with lower thermal conductivity
have also been created to replace aluminium and other highly conducting materials.
GFRP covered by Appelfeld in 2015 [3], was used as window frame material.
5.7.1
GFRP window frame
The first investigation was to find the U-value of the window frame. Therefore the model
with frame and glazing was modelled for heat transfer. The approach was to conduct
the one-two method involving two types of modelling. The first part was to find the
U-value of the frame (Uf) by modelling the glazing part as an insulation panel. The
next part was to find the thermal bridge in the glazing-frame connection. The insulation
panel was therefore replaced with a glazing consisting of spacer profile, glass panes and
cavities. For this model, the default choice of two-box method was used to categorise as
a default option on the current market.
Table 5.28: Geometrical dimensions for window frame model.

Glazing height [m]
Frame width [m]
0.04365

0.105
Figure 5.39: Temperature distribution of reference quadruple-glazed window.
99
==PAGE:120==
5 Heat transfer of analysis of windows
The thermal conductance (L2D) through the window frame model was found along
the heat transmission from one temperature to another.
The first part assumed an
insulation panel as glazing, which meant a thermal conductivity of 0.035 W/mK. The
height of the panel (hp) was the same as the glazing height shown in Table 5.28, the same
thing concerns the height of the frame (hf). The U-value of the frame could thereby be
calculated as in Equation 5.6.
Uf = L2D −Uphp
hf
(5.6)
The next part involved replacing the insulation with a glazing unit.
The target
was to find the linear loss between the glazing unit and the window frame. The same
geometrical lengths were used. The linear thermal transmittance (Ψl) was calculated
by applying the the U-values and heights of the frame and glazing respectively as in
Equation 5.7.
Ψl = L2D −Ufhf −Ughg
(5.7)
Lastly, the thermal bridge was used to calculate the total U-value of the window
frame.
Uf,tot = Uf + Ψl
hf
(5.8)
The results shown in Table 5.29 show relatively low U-values for the GFRP frame.
The smaller size and use of GFRP as frame material combined with pockets of EPDM
could produce a stable window frame with an acceptable U-value. The best window
frames on the current market reach around 2.3-2.6 W/m2K which indicates that reference
model is a great improvement if window manufacturers would decide to produce it.

CHAPTER 6
Modelling of window
designs & building
All work addressed and presented in chapter 3, chapter 4 and chapter 5 was used in this
chapter to create the modelling used for the future chapters. The modelling is primarily
done in the simulation program ”IDA ICE 4.8 Expert Edition”. It involved the creation
of models with the different window concepts and its properties.
6.1
Design of simulation model
The prime focus was to simulate the windows with its specific attributes.
A model
resembling an one floor residential building in Denmark was used. It was inspired by
a real new building documented in the SBI report ‘Cost‐optimal levels of minimum
energy performance requirements in the Danish Building Regulations’ in 2013 [1]. The
Building model used, was already created for the DTU course Sustainable Buildings.
The 3D model is shown in Figure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: 3D overview of IDA ICE model.
As shown in Figure 6.2, the model resembles an family house with 2 children bed-
rooms, one master bedroom, 3 bathrooms, a kitchen, a living room and a utility room.
==PAGE:124==
6 Modelling of window designs & building
The internal area of the building measures 150 m2, with a rectangular shape measur-
ing in length about 20 m and a width of 8 m. The rectangular side of the building is
orientated towards South and North.
Figure 6.2: Floor plan of simulated building in IDA ICE.
The model is based on an one-store residential building designed to meet the Danish
Building Regulations 2018 (BR18) for low energy buildings. This meant that primary
energy factors of 1.8 and 0.6 were used for electricity and district heating, respectively.
The model was also designed to meet or aim to meet the indoor environment require-
ments of Category II in the European Building Standard DS/EN 15251. This include
the daylight requirement of 3%, in the middle of the room and an average of 2.1%, which
will be covered in chapter 7.
6.1.1
Building envelopes
The building parts in IDA ICE can only be defined as one dimensional layers. The
wall was designed with three inhomogeneous layers, an outer layer of 120 mm solid
brick and interior layer of 100 mm Ligth Weight Concrete (L/W) with 300 mm mineral
wool in between. The prefabricated wooden structure with columns in both vertical
and horizontal direction was assumed to be present in the insulation part. The ground
floor was made of Concrete to accommodate floor heating in the floor and with 370
mm Expanded Polystyrene (EPS) as insulation. The thermal transmittance (U-value)
of the building envelopes without considering thermal bridges, is presented in Table 6.1.
The building was considered a light-weight building which meant that the heat capacity
of the L/W concrete was reduced from 1050 J/kgK to 262.5 J/kgK to resemble the
thermal accumulation of 25 mm gypsum without changing the U-value. The detailed
description of the building envelope with layers, thicknesses and calculations can be
found in Appendix B.
104
==PAGE:125==
6.2 Internal gains and occupancy
Table 6.1: Thickness and thermal transmittance of building envelopes.

The thermal bridges were also considered in the modelling. The connection between
the external and internal wall was considered to have a default thermal bridge of 0.1
W/mK. The thermal bridge between the Roof and external wall was set to 0.1 W/mK.
According to Weitzmann in his study from 2015, a value of 0.125 W/mK between the
external slab and the external wall would be a good estimation for a wall-foundation
with a floor insulation of 350 mm [42].
6.2
Internal gains and occupancy
Occupancy in the family house was assumed to be 5 family members with 2 adults (par-
ents) and 3 children. The Schedule was created to present the daily activity, absence
and sleeping pattern of a typical working family. The moisture production was also de-
fined, specifically caused by activities such as washing, shower and also by the occupants
themselves.

==PAGE:126==
6 Modelling of window designs & building
The internal gains was set to a constant value of 5 W/m2 as described in Danish
Standard BR15. The internal gains include human activity, various housing equipment
and lighting everything that constitutes some kind of heat gain. In this case, the internal
gains was divided into two categories, Occupancy and Equipment.
The internal gains had to be divided into to the two categories to both accurately
resemble the internal gains and avoid over and under-dimension values. The total inter-
nal gains for each room corresponding to 5 W/m2.
The total energy produced by the defined occupants in the program was extracted
by a simulation. The results were converted from kWh to W/m2, this value was used to
find the heat gains caused by the Equipment by taking the difference between the total
of 5 W/m2 and from occupant’s metabolic rate. The heat gaint division was therefore
set according to the schedule in Table 6.2, which meant that the heat gain would vary
from time to time. The heat gains from equipment would equal 5 W/m2 during time
periods outside the defined schedules.

6.3
Building services
The model was designed with the appropriate building services and its settings. The
building services were defined with settings and structured to be well functional and
capable of providing a satisfactory indoor climate with low energy use.
106
==PAGE:127==
6.3 Building services
6.3.1
Heating & Cooling system
The rooms were assigned an ideal heater to resemble the perfect floor heating and cool-
ing system. The heating set-point was set at 20◦C and the cooling set-point at 27◦C,
this informed the program that the desired room temperature should be in this indoor
temperature range, thereby calculating the energy required to keep it there.
6.3.2
Infiltration & ventilation system
The infiltration rate of the rooms was assumed to be wind-driven with a flow of 0.5
l/(s.m2) per floor area at a pressure difference of 50 Pa. The pressure coefficients from
wind on the surfaces of the building model were automatically defined by using the
option ”Semi-Exosed” in IDA ICE.
Two different types of ventilation systems was created, one for the models with fixed
windows and one for the openable sliding windows. Both models had an additional
mechanical ventilation system with a ground loop to provide satisfactory Indoor Air
Quality (IAQ) and save energy by using cool energy from the ground.
6.3.2.1
Mechanical ventilation system
A mechanical ventilation system was implemented, by defining an Air Handling Unit
(AHU) for the whole building. The air flow of the ventilation system was set to 0.3
l/s/m2, specified according to the required specific air flow from BR18. The floor area
of the building was 150 m2, which meant a total air flow in the building of 45 l/s. The
illustration of the set up and model of the AHU in IDA ICE is shown in Figure 6.3.
Figure 6.3: Overview of AHU and components.
107
==PAGE:128==
6 Modelling of window designs & building
The ventilation system was designed with a system type Variable Airflow Volume
(VAV). This meant a variation of the supplied airflow during specified periods. In the
fan settings, ASHRAE stnd. 90.1 (2007) app. G was selected as VAV part load perfor-
mance, with a rated flow of 45 l/s as the total air flow in the building.
The build up of the AHU consisted of two fans with a Specific Fan Power (SFP)
of 0.4 kW/(m3/s) and Efficiency of 0.6 (60%) for each. This gave a total SFP of 0.8
kW/(m3/s) corresponding to a value of 800 J/m3.
The ventilation system was only
designed to provide satisfactory Indoor Air Quality (IAQ), this meant that the heating
and cooling coil was ”shut off” in the model. The only component to control the air
temperature was set to be the Heat Exchanger, defined with a heat recovery efficiency
of 85% and a maximum flow of 0.045 m3/s (45 l/s). The ventilation system had flexible
ducts and one manifold with 8 duct attachments. The system strategy was created to
accommodate supplying air to Dryrooms such as bedrooms, living room and extract
air from Wetrooms such as bathroom, kitchen with a significant buildup of moisture
and air particles. The system had 8 air terminals for supply and exhaust in respective
rooms.

==PAGE:129==
6.4 Natural ventilation
6.4
Natural ventilation
A simple passive system with natural ventilation was created to ”operate” together with
mechanical system. Both the ventilation hatch and sliding window were designed to be
openable parts in the rooms to have enough air change. Both the hatch and sliding
window was created in IDA ICE as a glazing unit. The main difference was that the
hatch was defined with a minimal Light Transmission and g-value (close to 0) but given
a U-value corresponding to the centre U-value. The sliding window was defined with
the glazing properties. The area size was designed to provide a minimum air-change of
0.5 h−1 and a maximum of 6 h−1 when fully opened.
6.4.1
Schedule & settings
Figure 6.4 show the setup of the controller system used for opening of the ventilation
hatch. The controller is composed of three stages of settings for different periods, sum-
mer period, transition period and winter period.
Figure 6.4: Overview of Hatch and ventilation controller system.
Table 6.5 show the defined settings for the opening of a ventilation hatch or sliding
window during certain periods. The controller system run on temperature set points.
The opening of the hatch and sliding window is based on signals which tells the system
to open by a fraction of the area, where 0 is fully closed and 1 is full open hatch or
window area.
109
==PAGE:130==
6 Modelling of window designs & building
Table 6.5: Opening controller for Ventilation Hatch and Sliding window.

C
15 June - 01 Sep
Proportional
24 / 22 ◦C
1.0
0.3
Rule A - Default
During this period the heating is activated and the hatches/window are fully closed as
the system is programmed to only rely on the mechanical system to provide a satisfactory
IAQ. The hatch or window will only open when exceeding 25◦C during periods outside
the colder periods of a typical year.
Rule B - Transition period
The transition from summer to winter involves a slow modification of the operating
system, as the determining factors slowly changes. The opening of the ventilation hatch
or window was still in practice, programmed to fully open when exceeding 25◦C but now
completely close when the indoor temperature drops below 23◦C. The reminder of the
time all ventilation is provided by the mechanical ventilation. The transition period is
necessary to avoid negative interaction between the systems.
Rule C - Summer period
The upper box represent the opening of the ventilation hatch during summer periods,
15 June to 01 September. During this period all heating and mechanical ventilation is
deactivated and the only ”system” operating is the ventilation hatch or sliding window.
No energy is used as the hatch and opening of a window is a passive system and de-
signed to be opened by the occupants in certain degrees depending on the temperature
conditions inside and outside, to avoid overheating and/or draught. During the period
the hatch or window is always open at a minimum of 30% when the temperature drops
below 22◦C.
110
==PAGE:131==
6.5 Modelling of window models
6.5
Modelling of window models
The windows were modelled with a calculated area size in relation to a optimal daylight
contribution of 2.1% (or 300 lux) in the rooms. This was included by defining the glazing
area as in Equation 7.2. The formula states that the total glazing area should be at least
17% of the floor area of a room and that the default LT value is 75% which is divided
with the LT of the glazing to give a factor that considers the difference in LT.
Ag = Afloor · 17% · 75
LTw
(6.1)
IDA ICE lacks the function to define frameless windows as it demands the definition
of a ”frame around” the edges of the glazing. An extremely slim frame although in
reality not present, was assumed to be 1E-10 m thin thereby presenting minimal heat
losses. The final window area was calculated by including the thickness. The frame
was defined as a part of the wall with the same U-value. In that way, IDA ICE would
recognise it as a wall and not consider any additional heat losses than already present
in the walls.
6.5.1
Window size
The window size was determined according to requirements and/or recommendations
used by manufacturers to create realistic window dimensions for the building simulation
models. The height of the glazing was fixed at 1.1 m for all models, as the daylight is
at its greatest with increasing width and less with height of a window. This meant that
the main variation in size was the glazing width. The number of windows in a room
was given according to the glass area and maximum width but also to make sure that
sufficient daylight would be present in all regions of the rooms regardless of size.
The window size was limited due to the large weight caused by having quadruple
glass panes. It was necessary to dimension the glazing units with reasonable dimensions
without causing any static problems to the window frame or wall for the frameless win-
dows. The glazing size was designed in accordance to the specific guidelines provided
by the window manufacturer Velfac and Pilkington. It was desired to use 4 mm glass
panes as the use of thicker and heavier glass panes would cause larger static forces along
the increase of cost and lower light transmittance through the glazing units.
As the height was of the glazing was fixed, the glazing width was dimensioned by
addressing the curves in Figure 6.5. The maximum width of the frameless windows were
set to 2.5 m according to the graph from pilkington. The models with window frame
were dimensioned according to Velfac’s recommendations with a maximum width of 2
m to assure that the window frame construction would be able to withstand the load.
111
==PAGE:132==
6 Modelling of window designs & building
Figure 6.5: Window size according to thickness of glass panes, from Pilkington Glas-
fakta 2015.
6.5.2
Glazing properties & linear losses
The glazing properties in IDA ICE was defined under glazing construction, the parame-
ters were given as centre U-value of glazing, LT, g-value and ST according to the results
from chapter 3. All properties were based on real products and can be found in Ap-
pendix D.
The total losses around the glazing include the thermal linear losses around all edges.
The linear losses were taken from chapter 5. The definition of the thermal bridge in IDA
ICE was given as the total linear loss around the external perimeter of the window to
the wall construction. An weighted arithmetic mean of the linear losses was calculated
to find a total value, which would consider both horizontal and vertical losses around
all edges of the window in relation to the dimensions. The total weighted linear loss
(Ψtot,w) was expressed mathematically as Equation 6.2. The total average linear loss was
calculated by taken the average of the weighted arithmetic mean for each units per room.
112
==PAGE:133==
6.5 Modelling of window models
Ψw,tot =
∑n
i=edge Ψi · li
∑n
i=edge li
=>
∑n
i=edge Ψi · li
Ptot
(6.2)
Where Ψi is the linear loss at a edge and li is the length/width of that edge. The
summation of the edges becomes the perimeter (P) of the whole construction. This
makes sure that the linear loss is weighted according both the linear loss and the length
in relation to the total Perimeter (Ptot).
Calculating the weighted average thermal linear loss was done for each models and
more specifically done as explained below. The detailed calculations can be found in
Appendix E.
• Fixed window with ventilation Hatch - Linear losses from top, bottom and
sides for both.
• Sliding window - Linear losses from top, bottom, sides between window and wall
and connection between sliding and fixed part of window.
• Reference windows - Linear losses around the perimeter of the frame dimensions
of the windows.
Figure 6.6: Illustration of weighting linear losses.
The windows were the central part of all models, which meant that the only changes
in the simulation models were related to the windows and/or glazing. The remaining
part of the chapter explains the definition of the different window concepts.
6.5.3
Frameless window-hatch
The frameless sealed windows were created according to previously described procedure
with geometry, glazing properties and definition of the thermal bridge caused by the

==PAGE:134==

window-wall connection. The models were created with a ventilation hatch to provide
natural ventilation according to the occupants’ need. The size of the ventilation hatch
was fixed at a height of 1.6 m and width of 0.5 m but the size could be changed. This
would help accommodate enough space for evacuation. The window was created with
either the ventilation hatch placed next to the window or above it as shown in Figure 6.7.
The height of the glazing was at default set to 1.1 m with a variable width with the
possible to reach 3 m as the wall construction is capable of carrying the load from the
window and ventilation hatch. The hatch was dimensioned to be big enough to provide
enough ventilation without causing draught and provide an exit in case of fire.

and ventilation hatch units. The linear loss was similar for all rooms but with some
minor differences. This meant that an average value could be taken for all rooms to
represent a total average linear loss for the whole building.

Reference models
Two reference window models with window frame were also created for comparison. The
two models were created to portray the best triple glazed and quadruple glazed window

Pilkington Spectrum. The aim was again to find a glazing with the highest possible
LT and lowest possible U-value. A window frame was used for both cases which also
meant open able windows. The total U-value of the window frame was found to be 1.66
W/m2K with a slim window frame thickness of 44 mm.

6.6
Investigations
The next two chapters involves the IDA ICE simulations to find the relevant results for
Indoor Climate and Energy use in the building. The investigations were divided into
following:
• Influence of different glazing types and coating on overheating, thermal conditions
• The thermal conditions and use of natural ventilation with a ventilation hatch or
sliding window
• The influence of linear losses to the total energy use of a building compared to
models with window frame
• Comparison of influence of different window designs to space heating and total
primary energy use

==PAGE:139==
CHAPTER 7
Indoor climate
7.1
Design criteria for indoor climate
The requirements and recommendations were based on those of the European building
code EN15251:2007. The indoor climate results were examined according to require-
ments, the preferred criteria was to meet category II.
7.1.1
Operative temperature
The parameter used to validate the thermal conditions was the operative temperature.
It is the combined influence of ambient air temperature and mean radiant temperature
in a room.. Although the accurate explanation would be ”uniform temperature of an
enclosure in which an occupant would exchange the same amount of heat by radiation
plus convection as in the existing non-uniform environment ” [21].
To = hc · Ta + ¯hr + ¯Tr
hc + hr
(7.1)
Where
Ta is the air temperature,[◦C]
¯Tr is the mean radiant temperature, [◦C]
hc is the heat transfer coefficient by convection, [W/m2K]
hr is the heat transfer-transfer coefficient by radiation, [W/m2K]
The greatest temperature concern was hours of of overheating in the rooms. The most
affected rooms were the south and eastern orientated rooms, exposed to morning and
afternoon sun. As a residential building is used for analysis, the overheating temperature
was changed from 26◦C to 27◦C. Another concern was temperature below 20◦C, although
quite uncommon but could occur at some junctures during colder periods.
Table 7.1: Temperature requirements.
Temperature below 20◦C
Temperature above 27◦C
Requirement
≈0 Hours
< 100 Hours
==PAGE:140==
7 Indoor climate
7.1.2
Indoor Air Quality (IAQ)
The Indoor Air Quality (IAQ) was determined by the CO2 concentration in the building
along the levels of Relative Humidity (RH). The requirements of the CO2 concentration
and RH levels was desired to meet at minimum for most of the time category II. This
meant a CO2 below 900 ppm and RH between 25-60% to avoid moisture problems such
as condensation and mould growth.
However, it was not considered relevant as the
outcome of IAQ was largely determined by ventilation and not the windows itself. This
meant that the IAQ results in this project were neglected.
7.2
Daylight
The default level of LT to achieve a desired amount of sunlight is 75%. The daylight
requirement according to the Danish Building regulation 2018 (BR18) is minimum 2.1%
in daylight factor (DR) or an illuminance of 300 lux in the ”occupied space”. Another way
is to dimension the glass surface without shadowing effect to an equivalent of minimum
10% of the floor area according to BR18 [5]. The glazing area was dimensioned according
to equation 7.2 which expresses the required glazing area of a window to meet the
requirements. This would guarantee enough daylight. The daylight results were not
part of the main content but can be found in Appendix E.
Ag = Afloor · 17% · 75
LTw
(7.2)
7.3

coatings
The first investigation was to see the influence of different glazing types and glass coating
on temperature. Different types of coated glazing units were investigated. The models
in IDA ICE were given windows dimensioned after Equation 7.2 to consider both LT
and room area. All windows were given the same optical and thermal properties, which
meant that a model was created for each of the coated glazing units in Table 7.2.

7.3 Investigation of glazing with different coatings
7.3.1
Thermal conditions
The temperature results of each room were gathered and presented as a Cumulative
Distribution Function (CDF) for each of the six models. The temperatures were given
as fraction of time with meant a whole year of 8760 hours.
Figure 7.2: Temperatures of Optiwhite and Optitherm S1 N .
Figure 7.3: Temperatures of Optitherm S3 OW and Suncool 70/40.
123
==PAGE:144==
7 Indoor climate
Figure 7.4: Temperatures of K Glass N OW and Suncool 70/35.
The investigation demonstrated that overheating never exceeded 100 hours and far
from it. The highest hours of overheating was experienced with the use of Optiwhite as
outer glass pane. The living room and dining room with three and two windows respec-
tively and orientated towards the south were exposed to large amount of solar radiation.
This meant that these rooms were subject to largest risk of overheating, as illustrated
by Table 7.6. Overheating in the rooms was more frequent for cases with higher g-values
in the glazing units. The worst case of overheating was seen in a building with the use
of K-Glass N OW with a g-value of 0.48 and least case of overheating was with rooms
equipped with Suncool 70/35 with a g-value of 0.33.
Another influence to the final results was the balance between a high LT and g-
value. Gazing units with high LT helped provide great amount of daylight without very
large windows which in return meant smaller area of glass exposed to solar radiation.
Although as seen from the results, overheating in some degrees were still present but
acceptable in terms of BR18 requirements. The glazing units with lower LT on the other
hand, were larger in area but the size was compensated by low g-values which meant
low overheating as reflected by Table 7.6.
The results could indicate the possibility of implementing thermally insulated glazing
units in all parts of a building, instead of relying on solar coated glazing units in rooms
subject to large amount of solar radiation. Therefore prioritising high LT values in the
glazing units to therefore maximise the daylight intake and keep the size to reasonable
level. It could be a change in direction of designing glazing units, as the importance of
LT could spearhead the design of windows to be even more focused on daylight intake
with smaller glazing area than solar coated glazing units with larger glazing area.
124

7.3.2
Airflow rates
The glazing size area together with smart opening function defined for the ventilation
hatch were the two main reasons that the overheating was maintained below the maxi-
mum required 100 Hours. This meant that another result had to be investigated, mainly
the airflow rates reached with the ventilation hatch. A maximum airflow rate of 6 Air
Change per Hour (ACH) should be an appropriate estimate to avoid or reduce risk for
draught caused by airflows. Although in some cases during warm periods, occupants
would normally prefer constant ventilation by opening a window or hatch to keep the
indoor conditions comfortable.
The airflow rates were calculated for each room in the model. IDA ICE conducted
airflow calculation based on the the defined wind pressure conditions outside the building
and airflows by bouyancy forces 2. The airflows were recalculated from l/s to Air Change
per Hour (ACH) as shown in Equation 7.3.
ACH = 3.6 · qroom
Vroom
(7.3)
2Bouyancy forces - temperature driven airflows depedent on the air density

7.4
Sliding window
The glazing unit design was based on the results of previous investigation. This meant
the the glazing unit referred to as Optitherm S3 OW was used for this investigation
and the other remaining ones.
The same investigation was made as in the previous cases but the glazing unit with
ventilation hatch was replaced with two windows, one representing the fixed window and
the other the sliding window. Both windows were considered frameless which meant a
very thin frame thickness in IDA ICE. Unlike the frameless window-hatch solution, both
the sliding and fixed window were given same glazing properties as both units were
considered to be the full window. The fraction of opening the sliding part was set at
100% for all rooms. The dimensions of the window and parts is presented in Table 7.8.

7.5
Reference frame windows
The third solution to improve the energy performance of windows was to instead of
constructing frameless windows, optimise and improve the window frame. Three models
representing different types of window frame were designed to investigate the overheating
and airflow rates. The same opening function defined in chapter 6 was used but slightly
modified due to the larger size of the windows compared to the hatch. The window
opening was limited by applying a fraction of opening to each window units in IDA ICE.
The fraction of opening and window opening area per window of each room is illustrated
in Table 7.10. The same setting applied for both reference models and the conventional
frame model.
