CHAPTER 8
Energy use
Energy analysis of building models influenced by different glazing and window design
8.1
Energy use criteria
The energy consumption criteria was defined according to the Danish Building Regula-
tion ”Bygningsreglement 2018” (BR18). The building models were designed to fulfil the
energy requirements of 2020 (BR20) of as aforementioned in chapter 6. The energy con-
sumption of a building is described by the primary energy which reflects factors applied
to encourage the use of more sustainable form of energy sources. The primary energy
factors according to BR20 is presented in Table 8.1.
Table 8.1: Primary energy factors BR2020.
District heating
Electricity
Other heat sources
Primary
energy factor
0.6
1.8
1
The energy consumption requirements for 2020 were updated and changed to be
voluntary and not mandatory as firstly planed. The energy value itself was changed
from 20 kWh/m2 to 27 kWh/m2 [5]. The main target of the project was to prove the
possibility of creating a residential building capable of providing satisfactory indoor
climate and have an energy consumption below 20 kWh/m2. All models were therefore
designed to meet the old 2020 energy requirement.
8.2
Energy consumption
Six different models were created and simulated to find the space heating. The remaining
parts constituting the total energy use of the building models were constant and therefore
not of interest. Nevertheless, the total primary energy was calculated from all parts,
including heating, DHW and HVAC. Several investigations were made to simulate and
compare the primary energy consumption of each of following models:
==PAGE:154==
8 Energy use
• Conventional window model - a window model with a conventional frame with
a U-value of 2.32 W/m2K and frame thickness of 100 mm. This model resembles
the conventional window product available on the market, this implies a triple
glazing.
• Improved conventional model - a window frame model with a U-value of 2.63
W/m2K and the same frame thickness of 44 mm as the reference frame. The idea
was to model the most energy efficient triple glazing with the current best window
frame on the market.
• Reference triple - a window frame model with triple glazing as the previously
described model but with the optimised GFRP frame (U-value of 1.66 W/m2K)
made for triple glazing.
• Reference quadruple - optimised GFRP window frame model with a slim frame
of 44 mm and a U-value of 1.66 W/m2K with quadruple glazing unit. The improved
window frame model if a window frame is absolutely needed.
• Sliding window - the partly fixed and partly openable frameless window designed
as described in previous chapters.
• Frameless window-hatch - fixed frameless quadruple glazing unit with an im-
proved ventilation hatch solution integrated together with the glazing and wall
construction.
• It should be noted to that the same glazing units with the same properties as
used in chapter 7 were used for the models. This meant ”Optitherm S3 OW”
as quadruple glazing units and”OptiClear” for triple glazing units. The frame
window frame models were given solar coated glazing, Suncool 70/35 in the living
room as done in chapter 7 to avoid overheating.
The total linear loss for each model was calculated to compare it between the different
models. The total linear loss was calculated from the frame and the weighted average
linear loss as in Equation 8.1. See section A.2 for calculations and values.
Ψtot = Uf,tot · frame thickness + Ψtot,w
(8.1)

8.2 Energy consumption
One model was simulated for the frameless window-hatch, with quadruple glazing.
The sliding window was simulated as triple and quadruple glazed, to see the influence
the glazing units have on the space heating. The reference windows were also simu-
lated with both a triple glazing and quadruple glazing. The two conventional window
models described in subsection D.3.2 were also simulated. This would give an overall
energy comparison between the frameless windows, improved windows and conventional
ones, likewise compare the effect of replacing triple glazing with quadruple glazing units.
As reflected by the energy results of Table 8.3, it clearly stands out that all except
the conventional frame model, met an primary energy use below 20 kWh/m2. The main
difference between the different models are clear by comparing the space heating. The
results show that replacing the improved conventional frame and triple glazing with the
reference frame and quadruple glazing unit could help reduce the space heating by 26%
(413 kWh). Whereas replacing the triple glazing with quadruple with both having a
reference window frame could reduce the space heating by 15% (202 kWh).

The frameless windows indicates that replacing the window frame with an incorpo-
ration of the glazing units directly to the wall could reduce the energy use even further.
The difference between the sliding window and frameless window-hatch was small, only
separated by 83.8 kWh. Comparing the frameless models to the window frame model on
the other hand demonstrates the influence window frame have on the space heating due
to heat losses. The sliding window managed to reduce space heating by 12% compared
to the reference model with quadruple glazing. Whereas the use of frameless window
with ventilation hatch could reduce space heating by 5% compared to the use of the
Reference window frame.
135
==PAGE:156==
8 Energy use
The sliding window was also investigated with a triple glazing unit in case the window
manufacturer would decide that. The space heating saw an increase of 11% (133 kWh)
for replacing the quadruple glazing with a triple glazing. The sliding window (triple)
compared to the reference models, was shown to be almost equal to the reference win-
dow with quadruple glazing. On the other hand, the triple glazed sliding window would
have a space heating 15% lower than the triple reference window. This would indicate
that the most feasible option in terms of energy consumption would be quadruple glazed
windows or the frameless triple glazed sliding window.
The greatest difference was shown by comparing the space heating achieved with
the two frameless window designs to the conventional/traditional window design. The
sliding window with quadruple glazing and the frameless window-hatch could reduce the
space heating compared to the current best window product on the market by 53% and
50% respectively. The results indicate the potential to both move from triple glazing
to quadruple glazing but foremost reconsider the current window designs.
Both the
optimised window frame but especially the frameless window show large decrease of
space heating which would make it easier to construct Nearly Zero Energy Buildings
(NZEB). The next part of the project involves the economical benefits based on the
presented energy results and the insulation optimisation.
136
==PAGE:157==
CHAPTER 9
Economics
Adresses the economical benefits of the different window types and how the buildings can
be economically optimised due to improvement of the window energy performance.
9.1
Current situation
One important aspect is as always with every product. The economical prospects. The
current window industry works as the simplified process presented in Figure 9.1. Firstly
the glass panes are created with its respective coatings depending on what the costumer
would desire to have in terms of function. The next part involves the creation of a glazing
unit by the glazing manufacturer who usually is the same glass pane manufacture. The
glass panes are put together and aligned with a spacer profile. Sealant is therefore used
to keep the unit together in one and other features as desiccant and others may be
implemented for moisture protection and so on. The glazing unit is sent to the window
manufacture who constructs the window frame to make it possible to open the window
in different ways. The window is finalised as a product. The last step is to incorporate
the window into a wall of a building.
Figure 9.1: Window production and process.
==PAGE:158==
9 Economics
The goal for future modern windows is to skip the third part of the process, win-
dow manufacturing.
If the window is frameless, the glazing unit can be specifically
designed for direct application to the wall construction. The energy consumption can
be significantly reduced if the window frame is removed as demonstrated in chapter 8.
9.2
Potentials
The price of windows with multi-layer glazing can be high due to the increase of price in
relation to number of glass panes. The total cost of incorporating window to a building
would therefore be significant part of a construction budget. The target of the project
was therefore to create cheaper possibilities of constructing windows. The most expen-
sive part of the industry is to create the final window, which is why a frameless window
could reduce cost and time in the creation of windows.
The window frame structure is despite its small size, a large source of heat losses
in buildings. The current market of window frame includes highly conducting window
frame materials such as wood and aluminium. The main problem has been to find a
window frame material with excellent thermal properties and in the same time, capable
structural abilities.
The first step could be the creation of an optimised window frame made from GFRP
as the presented reference model.
The frame is thinner and have a better thermal
performance than other products on the current market. The desired option would be
the creation of frameless windows, thereby potentially help reduce the production time
by skipping the window frame and furthermore produce smart and aesthetic products
with lower cost.
9.3
Cost of Conserved Energy
As detailed information about window production and cost could be classified infor-
mation and the as the economical information about the industry is in current state
non-present or difficult to attain. The most simple way to determine the economical
benefits of the window models was to investigate the window performance with respect
to Cost of Conserved Energy (CCE).
The approach includes a calculation of the marginal energy saving price for floor,
exterior wall and roof, providing an economical overview when adding more thickness
of a given insulation in an envelope component. With this method, a final solution bal-
anced between cost and amount of insulation can help avoid over-dimensioning of the
insulation and still fulfil the energy requirements of the proposed building regulation.
138
==PAGE:159==
9.3 Cost of Conserved Energy
In this way, it will be possible to choose a final solution that is balanced between
each envelope component in terms of insulation thickness within a given boundary. This
would help avoiding possible imbalances between costs, resulting in finding an optimal
insulation thickness having the same cost per kWh (CCE). The final solution should be
in balance from an economic standpoint [32] and as for this investigation reach a total
primary energy around 20 kWh/m2.
The basic definition of CCE is described by Equation 9.1
CCE = a(n, d) · Imeasure
∆Eyear
(9.1)
where, Imeasure is the investment cost, or additional cost, of an energy-conserving
measure (in a monetary unit) and Eyear is the annual energy conserved by the measure
(in this case kWh). The factor a(n,d) is the capital recovery rate defined as shown in
Equation 9.2
a(n, d) =
d
1 −(1 + d)−n
(9.2)
where, d is the real interest rate (in absolute terms) and n is useful life time of
the measure (in years). This basic definition of CCE needs a number of supplementing
factors if it is to be applicable for the design of new buildings.
Table 9.1: Assumed parameters for CCE calculations.
Real interest
rate (d)
Reference
period (t)
Primary energy factor
for electricity
2.5%
100 years
1.8
For this project, the durability of the insulation material were set at 100 years which
is the reference period/investment period. If the interest rate was assumed to be 0 it
would therefore give a capital recovery rate (a(n,d)) of 1/100. The calculation with a
constant recovery rate of 1/100 was used to calculate the CCE.
The insulation materials used in the building was mineral wool in walls and roof and
Expanded Polystyrene (EPS) in floor. The cost of the two materials were accounted for
in the calculations. The price per volume of insulation material was 300 DKK/kWh for
mineral wool and 400 DKK/kWh for EPS.
The insulation thickness was varied to find the energy difference from the start insula-
tion to a ”smaller” thickness. The cost was determined by the insulation decrease which
was approximately 50 mm. In one of the cases for roof and floor the decrease of insulation
thickness from the default option was larger which was accounted for in the calculations.
The thermal bridge between the external slab and external wall varies in relation to
the insulation thickness in the floor. The larger the insulation thickness, the lower the
139
==PAGE:160==
9 Economics
thermal bridge would be and vice versa. The thermal bridge at each point of insulation
thickness was presented as a graph shown in Figure 9.2.
A function describing the
relation could be found from the graph and defined as ”y = -0.25X + 0.2” as illustrated
in Figure 9.2. The y represents the thermal bridge and the variable X represents the
insulation thickness. In this way an accurate thermal bridge could be calculated and
used for simulation when changing the insulation thickness of the floor when finding the
CCE.

9.3.1
Example of CCE analysis
The CCE analysis for all cases was done similarly which was therefore addressed by the
example using the sliding window model. All steps presented were done for all cases but
the detailed results can be found in Appendix F or the attached results.
The CCE analysis is composed of several steps involving the simulation of the space
heating in relation to different insulation thickness in the walls, roof and floor. The in-
sulation thickness is changed separately for each of the three construction parts, which
140
==PAGE:161==
9.3 Cost of Conserved Energy
emphasise that the insulation thickness for one e.g roof is changed while the thickness
for the others remain the same. This step is done for all construction parts until space
heating for all scenarios is found.
The space heating difference between the construction part with the default insu-
lation thickness and a lower thickness thickness was done, which thereafter continued
from the lower thickness to the next decrease until the limit of 100 mm insulation was
met. The space heating difference was used in combination with the insulation cost and
factorisation to find the CCE as done in Equation 9.1.

The building model with the optimised insulation thicknesses were simulated to find
the total primary energy. As reflected by Table 9.5, the total energy consumption of the
building was found to be 19.91 kWh/m2, close to the predefined energy demand of 20
kWh/m2. The results presented in Table 9.5 illustrates that it was possible to reduce
the insulation thickness by 13% (39 mm) in the walls, 27% (99 mm) in the roof and
51% (189 mm) in the floor. This would increase the space heating significantly from the
default models but on the other hand save loads of unnecessary insulation and thereby
reduce the insulation cost. Another aspect is the reduction of the construction parts in
size, leaving more space and a thinner building which is aesthetically preferable.

9.5 Window cost
The implementation of reference windows can reduce the insulation cost compared to
the use of conventional windows by 8% and 15% if triple and quadruple glazed reference
window was to be used. The lowest cost was found for the sliding window as expected
due to the largest reduction of insulation. The results in Table 9.11 indicates that re-
placing optimised conventional windows with the frameless windows could reduce the
total insulation cost by 22% and 20% for sliding window and frameless window-hatch,
respectively.
9.5
Window cost
Cost information of triple glazed windows from Rational were received by a window
manufacturers. The product list was attached to the appendices and can be found in
section D.3.
It was assumed for this case that all models were equipped with triple glazing but
maintained its glazing area and number of windows. Two fixed, non-openable windows
were given along two openable window examples. The properties for both models were
both similar, the main difference was the window frame.
The cost of the non-openable windows was somewhat proportional to the area for
both. However, this was not the case for the openable windows. A correlation between
the cost of the glazing and the area was found by attributing the reminder of the window
cost to a fixed price for the window frame. The dimensions of the windows are given in
Table 9.12.

Lastly, It should be stated that the investigation is based on pure assumptions. The
total cost of a window was provided by a manufacture, but the cost of the window frame
and glazing per area carry uncertainties. The comparison of the results were made to
give an idea of how the frameless windows could potentially be cost-beneficial for future
constructions. Other cost such as transport, service were not considered. The frameless
windows might require other measures during incorporation and the production of those
might exceed the cost of the glazing. Nevertheless, the comparison gives an indication
to the economical benefit of removing the window frame.

CHAPTER 10
Final model presentation
A presentation of the three new window models with detailed 3D illustration in different
states. The rendered 3D drawings were created with by Elizabeth Saliba in cooperation
with her project ”Nearly Zero Energy Buildings focusing on new types of windows”
10.1
Sliding window
The sliding window was modelled in 3D with its detailed components. A small part of
the wall was likewise modelled to illustrate the integration of the glazing units to the
wall.
Figure 10.1: 3D rendering of closed sliding window construction.
The sliding window was presented in closed and open state in Figure 10.1 and Fig-
ure 10.2, respectively. The sliding window was integrated to open in front of the fixed
==PAGE:170==
10 Final model presentation
glazing with small space between to avoid scratching the glass panes. It can be seen
from Figure 10.2 that the window was opened by sliding, leaving the air cavity in the
wall. The idea was to have a lever integrated to the sliding part of the window but
not included in the 3D model. The window was designed to have a large fixed window
for daylight and with a smaller sliding part to accommodate natural ventilation and a
potential fire escape.
Figure 10.2: 3D rendering of open sliding window construction.
The ”magnet connection” was as previously described, created to integrate the two
window parts together without dividing the window into two separate parts and avoid
the use of a slim wall in between. The aerogel insulation can be seen from the top of
both windows, with a small thickness of 22 mm. Judged from the figures, the design
can be said to acceptable for aesthetically reasons.
150
==PAGE:171==
10.2 Frameless window-hatch
Figure 10.3: Closed magnet connection.
Figure 10.4: Open magnet connection.
10.2
Frameless window-hatch
The frameless window-hatch model was integrated together by a similar idea as presented
in the sliding window. In between the two units, magnets were attached to the Glass
Fibre Reinforced Polyester (GFRP) and to the plastic profile around the hatch. This
connection would keep the units closed and attached together and in the wall. The hatch
in its fully opened state is seen in Figure 10.6, the opening of the hatch was done with
a traditional handle.
Figure 10.5: Window-hatch
model
in
closed state.
Figure 10.6: Window-hatch
with
open
hatch.
Figure 10.7 show the option to ”disassemble” the hatch and create a fire escape. The
first step was to open the insulated hatch part and thereafter proceed to open the filtered
part of the hatch. The dimensions were based on those described by BR18, minimum
width of 0.5 m and 1.1 m in height. In this project a larger height was used to make
it easier for all types of occupants to escape. However, the height can be varied by
potential manufacturers as long as the fire escape requirements are met.

The integration of the frameless window together with the ventilation hatch was
illustrated in detailed in the figures below. The hatch in closed state show the small
connection between the attached magnet on the hatch and glazing respectively. Similarly
Figure 10.10 show that the hatch will open as the magnet connection is ”loosened”
between the window and the hatch.
Figure 10.9: Closed magnet connection.
Figure 10.10: Open magnet connection.
10.3
Reference frame model
The optimised window frame model was created with a slim frame. Compared to other
windows, the window frame was slimmer and present a more aesthetic window frame
option.
The window was designed to be a pivot window, the window was therefore
opened from either top or bottom and closed in vice versa, only one part opened towards
the inside. This function would both ensure that window is easily opened and theft-proof.
The handle was placed on the upper part of the window.

==PAGE:175==
CHAPTER 11
Conclusion
The aim of the project was to create two frameless windows to reduce heat losses caused
by windows. Two frameless windows were designed and modelled. In addition to this,
an improved low energy window frame was created for quadruple glazing.
The first concept evolved around the idea of creating a fixed frameless window-hatch.
The window was incorporated into the wall instead of a window frame, combined with
a ventilation hatch providing a opening for natural ventilation and fire escape. The ven-
tilation hatch was integrated together with the window by applying sealant strip with
magnets between them. In this way a smart integration could ”unite” the window and
hatch into a single unit if needed. The default glazing unit of the structure was designed
to be quadruple glazed. The centre U-value of the quadruple glazing was 0.31 W/m2K
and 0.107 W/m2K for the ventilation hatch.
The second concept addressed the necessity of opening a window, regardless if it was
frameless or not. A frameless window with two glazing units was designed to resemble
a construction similar to that of a sliding door. One part was fixed to the wall, pro-
viding the main daylight intake. The other part was designed to be openabale element
opened by drawing a lever to slide it in front of the fixed part. The openable part was
integrated to the structural part of the top wall construction. The sliding window part
was designed to be large enough to provide enough ventilation and a possible fire escape.
The window could be designed as both triple and quadruple glazed.
In addition to the frameless window solutions, a model referred to as the Reference
window was created with an optimised slim window frame. The window was designed
to be a pivot window, which emphasise that the window opens inwards by drawing a
handle in the top of the window. The total U-value of the window frame was calculated
to be 1.66 W/m2K with a slim thickness of 44 mm. This was a significant improvement
compared to conventional window frame products. One of the best performing conven-
tional windwo frame available, had a total U-value of 2.63 W/m2K with the same frame
thickness of 44 mm, indicating the possibility to reduce the heat losses caused by the
window frame by 37%.
All three models were modelled to find the thermal linear transmittance between
the wall and the windows. Based on the default dimensions used for the windows or
glazing, an average weighted linear loss was calculated. The weighted average linear
loss considering all parts was found to be 0.043 W/mK for the frameless window-hatch,
0.039 W/mK for the sliding window and 0.023 W/mK for the Reference window.
==PAGE:176==
11 Conclusion
The glazing units were designed to have as high Light Transmission properties as
possible. Different coated glazing units were investigated to find a balance between the
Light Transmission and glazing area. The results indicated that overheating could be
prevented without solar coated glazing units. The high Light Transmission would utilise
the daylight intake while keep a reasonable glazing area. A combination of this and
an effective ventilation system, including a well programmed functioning between the
mechanical system and limited opening of windows or hatch would provide enough air-
flow to prevent overheating while avoiding draught. The maximum airflow rate before
causing draught was estimated to be 6 ACH, which was met for all cases.
The energy use was found and compared between the different models. The space
heating was addressed between the models. The frameless windows illustrated a big
difference to models with window frames. The results showed that replacing a conven-
tional window frame and triple glazing with the optimised window frame and quadruple
glazing presented as reference model, helped reduce the space heating by 29%. Whereas
replacing the triple glazing with quadruple glazing unit for the optimised window frame
model, could reduce space heating by 15%. The most significant results were found
when comparing a conventional window found on the current window market with the
frameless windows. The sliding window and frameless window-hatch models could re-
duce space heating by 53% and 50% compared to a conventional window with window
frame and triple glazing.
A Cost analysis of the insulation in the building models was made to compare the
impact, window designs would have on the insulation thickness. A default model with
370 mm insulation in the floor, 365 mm in the roof and 300 mm in the wall was used
for the comparison. The total insulation cost of the default building was about 70,4
thousand DKK. The improved conventional window model reduced the cost by 24%,
whereas the reference with triple and quadruple glazing reduced cost by 30% and 35%
respectively. The frameless windows managed to reduce the cost by 39% with the frame-
less window-hatch and 41% by using the quadruple glazed sliding window.
The window cost was addressed from a price list provided by a window manufacture.
The price was applied for a fixed window and an openable window. The window frame
price was assumed to be fixed and finding a correlation between the frame price and the
glazing area, a total cost could be applied to the fixed and openable windows. The total
window cost for a building could be reduced by 30% if a fixed window was implemented
as a frameless unit. Whereas, the cost could be reduced by 56% if the window frame of
a openable window was removed.
The results firmly indicates the possibility of using frameless window and increase
the number of glass panes to reduce heat losses and meet future energy requirements.
Furthermore, this could help reduce the time and cost in current window production.
156
==PAGE:177==
11.1 Future studies
11.1
Future studies
Future work should be focused on testing the presented concepts. Mock-ups would be
beneficial to address some potential structural concerns and if the idea of using mag-
netic seal and magnets as means of integration and opening/closing of the windows could
work. With the given dimensions and properties, prototypes of the frameless window-
hatch and sliding window would be ideal to prove the possibility of creating frameless
windows and dictate the process of production to the incorporation of those windows
into prefabricated walls.
Furthermore, it would be wise to research the acoustical properties of the frameless
windows and how those could differ from more conventional windows. As the windows
become more of a integrated part of wall construction, the structural vibration and
sound insulation would be of great interest to find out if it is worsened and/or in need
of optimisation or eventually an acoustical improvement.
Lastly, a more detailed investigation on the non-sealed window concept both frame-
less and with window frame should be made. The concept presents an interesting idea
with large potentials to both revolutionise window construction as known today and
reduce heat losses and cost. A study on the development of spacer profiles, attachments
and filtering of the non-sealed windows would be necessary to further the development
of non-sealed windows.

A.2 Total thermal transmittance comparison in section 8.2
A.2
Total thermal transmittance comparison
in section 8.2
The comparison of the weighted linear loss including window frame and weighted linear
losses around the window edges is presented here. The calculation was simply done by
multiplying the window frame with its thickness and summed together with the weighted
average between the window-wall connection, from chapter 6.
Table A.8: Calculation of total linear loss from window frame with linear losses around
window.
Frame

August 2014 – Nr. W6 – Änderungsindex 4-05/2016
Datenblatt Psi-Werte Fenster
auf Basis messtechnischer Ermittlung der äquivalenten Wärmeleitfähigkeit der Abstandhalter
ARBEITSKREIS ‘WARME KANTE’
Die äquivalente Wärmeleitfähigkeit wurde nach der ift-Richtlinie WA-17/1 “Wärmetechnisch verbesserte Abstandhal-
ter – Ermittlung der äquivalenten Wärmeleitfähigkeit durch Messung” ermittelt. Die damit berechneten repräsentativen 
linearen Wärmedurchgangskoeffizienten (repräsentative Psi-Werte) gelten für typische Rahmenprofile und Verglasun-
gen für die Ermittlung des Wärmedurchgangskoeffizienten UW von Fenstern. Sie wurden unter den in der ift-Richtlinie 
WA-08/3 „Wärmetechnisch verbesserte Abstandhalter – Teil 1: Ermittlung des repräsentativen Psi-Wertes für Fenster-
rahmenprofile“ festgelegten Rahmenbedingungen (Rahmenprofile, Verglasung, Glaseinstand, Rückenüberdeckung, 
Primär- und Sekundärdichtstoff) ermittelt. Diese Richtlinie regelt auch den Gültigkeitsbereich und die Anwendung der repräsentativen Psi-Werte. Zur Vermeidung von Run-
dungsfehlern wurden die Psi-Werte im Datenblatt auf 0,001 W/mK angegeben. Das Verfahren zur rechnerischen Bestimmung der Psi-Werte hat eine Genauigkeit von 
± 0,003 W/mK. Unterschiede von weniger als 0,005 W/mK sind nicht signifikant. Weitere Informationen sind dem Merkblatt 004/2008 “Kompass Warme Kante” 
des Bundesverband Flachglas zu entnehmen.
Ermittlung der Kennwerte durch:
 Edgetech Europe GmbH
Gladbacher Straße 23
D-52525 Heinsberg

December 2013 – No. W7 – Revision index 3-05/2016
‘WARM EDGE’ WORKING PARTY
The equivalent thermal conductivity has been determined in accordance with the ift guideline WA-17engl/1 “Thermally im-
proved spacers – Determination of the equivalent thermal conductivity by measurement”. The representative linear 
heat transfer coefficients calculated in this way (representative psi values) apply to typical frame profiles and glazing 
for the determination of the heat transfer coefficient UW of windows. They have been determined under the boundary 
conditions (frame profiles, glazing, glass mounting depth, back covering, primary and secondary sealant) defined in 
the ift guideline WA-08engl/3 “Thermally improved spacers – Part 1: Determination of the representative Psi value for 
window frame profiles”. This guideline also governs the area of validity and application of the representative psi values. In order to avoid rounding errors, the psi values in 
the data sheet have been given at 0.001 W/mK. The method for the arithmetical determination of the psi values has an accuracy of ± 0.003 W/mK. Differences of less 
than 0.005 W/mK are not significant. For further information, refer to the Bulletin 004/2008 “Guide to Warm Edge” of Bundesverband Flachglas.

± 0,003 W/mK. Unterschiede von weniger als 0,005 W/mK sind nicht signifikant. Weitere Informationen sind dem Merkblatt 004/2008 “Kompass Warme Kante” 
des Bundesverband Flachglas zu entnehmen.
Ermittlung der Kennwerte durch:

October 2014 – No. W26 – Revision index 2-10/2018
Data sheet Psi values for windows
based on determination of the equivalent thermal conductivity of spacers by measurement
‘WARM EDGE’ Working party
The equivalent thermal conductivity has been determined in accordance with the ift guideline WA-17engl/1 “Thermally im- 
proved spacers – Determination of the equivalent thermal conductivity by measurement”. The representative linear 
heat transfer coefficients calculated in this way (representative psi values) apply to typical frame profiles and glazing 
for the determination of the heat transfer coefficient UW of windows. They have been determined under the boundary 
conditions (frame profiles, glazing, glass mounting depth, back covering, primary and secondary sealant) defined in 
the ift guideline WA-08engl/3 “Thermally improved spacers – Part 1: Determination of the representative Psi value for 
window frame profiles”. This guideline also governs the area of validity and application of the representative psi values. In order to avoid rounding errors, the psi values in 
the data sheet have been given at 0.001 W/mK. The method for the arithmetical determination of the psi values has an accuracy of ± 0.003 W/mK. Differences of less 
than 0.005 W/mK are not significant. For further information, refer to the Bulletin 004/2008 “Guide to Warm Edge” of Bundesverband Flachglas.

APPENDIX E
Daylight data
E.1
Daylight simulations
Sufficient daylight intake was guaranteed by considering the glazing area to be 17% of
the floor area, while also consider a Light Transmission of 75 as the default value. The
daylight was found by conducting IDA ICE simulations. Figure E.1 show an example
for how the daylight intake would look in IDA ICE, the color marked the daylight levels.
Figure E.1: Illustration of daylight intake in simulation model with areas marked after
Daylight Factor.

Optitherm S1N

where glazing 4wC(74)-22Ar-S(3)4w-22Ar-S(3)4w-22Ar-S(3)4w was used in the living
room.
